<article id="post-1245" class="group post-container entry-content">
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">2014 M.F.A. Graduate Exhibition Opens Friday, May 16 @ BAM/PFA</h1>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		
			
		<p>Each year, BAM/PFA teams with the UC Berkeley Department of Art Practice to exhibit the work of their graduates in the galleries. This year’s graduates are: Joey Enos, Jason Fritz, Jessica Hankey, Helena Keeffe, Elizabeth Keegan, and Vreni Michelini Castillo. Be among the first to encounter the work of these <a href="http://bampfa.berkeley.edu/exhibition/mfa2014">six exceptional artists as they embark on their careers</a>.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>

	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->

</article><!-- #post-1245 -->

			
		
		</div><!-- #content-container -->


	</div><!-- #main -->

<article id="post-1434" class="group post-container entry-content">
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Four of our six MFA students have received the prestigious Murphy Cadogan award!</h1>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		
			
		<p>The Jack K. and Gertrude Murphy Fellowships, and Edwin Anthony and Adelaide Boudreaux Cadogan Scholarships help fuel the continued forward-thinking, visual arts movement that makes the Bay Area unique. Established in 1986, these awards are designed to further the development of Bay Area MFA students’ and to foster the exploration of their artistic potential in digital art, film/video, hybrid practice, installation, mixed media, painting, photography, and sculpture. Winners receive $5,000 in tuition assistance and have their work displayed in a professionally-curated exhibition at SOMArts Cultural Center. &#8211; See more at: <a href="http://sff.org/programs/awards-programs/art-awards/murphy-and-cadogan-contemporary-art-awards/#sthash.pE1YUtGs.dpuf">http://sff.org/programs/awards-programs/art-awards/murphy-and-cadogan-contemporary-art-awards/#sthash.pE1YUtGs.dpuf</a></p>
<p>The four MFA students who have received the award are:</p>
<p><em><strong>Leslie Dreyer</strong></em><br />
<em><strong>Tanja Geis</strong></em><br />
<em><strong>Lee Lavy</strong></em><br />
<em><strong>Matt Smith</strong></em></p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>

	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->

</article><!-- #post-1434 -->

<article id="post-1453" class="group post-container entry-content">
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">New Course Featuring Art Practice Greg Niemeyer: Sensing Cityscapes</h1>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		
			
		<p><a href="http://globalurbanhumanities.berkeley.edu/sensing-san-leandro-capturing-cityscapes-through-sensors">Professors <em>Greg Niemeyer (Art Practice)</em> and <em>Ron Rael (C.E.D.)</em></a> are co-teaching a course entitled <a href="http://globalurbanhumanities.berkeley.edu/academic-program"><em>SENSING CITYSCAPES: SENSORS, CITIES, POLICIES/BASIC PROTOCOLS FOR NEW MEDIA</em></a> in which students create and interrogate tools for collecting data on urban metabolisms. Through a suite of practical projects, the course introduces methods for retrieving and working with existing city data, investigating cities through surveys and mapping, generating data through digital sensing and ways of presenting data publicly. Students are actively partnering with the  City of San Leandro to find new ways of collecting and using data to improve city planning.</p>

	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->

</article><!-- #post-1453 -->

<article id="post-1548" class="group post-container entry-content">
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Congratulations to Undergrad Audrey Chen for Making it into the Juried Show &#8220;More Than One Way&#8221;</h1>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		
			
		<p>Congratulations to undergrad student Audrey Chen!</p>
<p>Audrey has made it into the prestigious juried exhibition at Southern Exposure Gallery, &#8220;<a href="https://www.soex.org/projects-exhibitions/more-one-way">More Than One Way</a>,&#8221; opening this Friday. This is indeed a great accomplishment.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>

<article id="post-1796" class="group post-container entry-content">
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">The grand opening exhibition at Embark Gallery, featuring three of our MFA students.</h1>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		
			
		<p>Michelle Ott, 2nd year</p>
<p>Matt Smith, 2nd year</p>
<p>Jose Figueroa, 1st year</p>
<p>* * * *</p>
<p>When Michelle Ott heard that a San Francisco gallery would be opening specifically to exhibit the visual art of Bay Area masters of fine arts students, she knew she had the right work for the first show.</p>
<p>Both the gallery and the exhibition would be called <em>Embark</em>, and Ott, a student in art practice at UC Berkeley, deduced that nobody else had embarked on a journey quite like hers, which was to fly 17 hours on four separate occasions to create art about Antarctica. She was right, and her enlarged photos and wall drawings get their own corner of the group show that opened Friday at Fort Mason. The show’s theme, “Embark,” may not be original, but Embark Gallery’s mission is so refreshingly obvious that it’s a wonder no curator or academic has thought of it before — pick an all-star team of art grad students and throw them together for a monthlong exhibition. Then do it all over again with a new theme, six times a year.</p>
<p>“We hope to create connections between these seven different MFA programs,” says Embark Gallery Director Angelica Jardini, who put out the call to UC Berkeley, California College of the Arts, Mills College, San Francisco Art Institute, San Francisco State, San Jose State and Stanford.</p>
<p>“There are so many innovative things going on at these somewhat isolated institutions,” Jardini says, “and we wanted to provide these students a professional space to show their work and also to get to know each other and to exchange ideas.”</p>
<p>Embark’s founder, Tania Houtzager, is an MFA student herself who got hit by a thunderbolt while managing the grad student gallery at the San Francisco Art Institute.</p>
<p>“It made me realize that so many of these programs don’t have opportunities to show your work before your final thesis show,” she says. “The only shows you can get are ones where you are competing with mid-career artists.”</p>
<p>These pre-career artists put some intriguing twists on the theme, which Jardini describes as “journeys, new beginnings, change of any kind. That was the prompt we gave the artists.” CCA student Omar Mismar’s twist was to create a red neon map of the routes he has taken while cruising men in San Francisco, using the Grindr mobile phone application.</p>
<p>“I would choose a guy on Grindr that I desired and would try to get as close as possible to him using the app,” says Mismar, in describing “The Path of Love #3,” which is front and center at the gallery entrance. Toward the back is SFAI student Matt Goldberg’s “Cadillac Treadmill,” made of a walking machine he found on Mission Street and tricked up with a front end in turquoise and a hood ornament. On the floor, looking real enough to trip over, is a parking curb in San Francisco livery, made of foam by Courtney Sennish, a student at CCA.</p>
<p><strong>&#8216;Phenomenal’</strong></p>
<p>“This is so phenomenal,” says Lee Gregory, a Fort Mason board member, watching the treadmill belt move at high speed. “What they are doing here is bringing the future of art to a great space.”</p>
<p>Houtzager, 26, and Jardini, 25, met at Sartle.com (See Art Differently), an art history database founded by Houtzager. The startup started up at Fort Mason, and in walking the halls they noticed a vacant room that was the perfect size for a gallery, 1,500 square feet, offering full western exposure to dramatic sunset views of the Golden Gate. They got the lease at a discounted rate and chose the name Embark because Fort Mason was the port of embarkation for 1.5 million troops headed into the Pacific Theater in World War II.</p>
<p>“This is an organization supporting emerging artists as they embark on their careers, and it’s very exciting,” says Nicholas Kinsey, director of external affairs for Fort Mason Center. “They wouldn’t be able to pay market-rate rent if they went out into the city, so we’re happy to have them here. This is our core mission, supporting the arts.”</p>
<p>The core mission of Embark is to showcase work from the seven local nonprofits, all of which are rated among the top 100 graduate programs in the country. Eliminated from consideration are the city’s two for-profit graduate programs, the Academy of Art University and the Art Institute of California — San Francisco.<br />
The gallery is supported by the Kabouter Foundation, which also funds Sartle.com. There won’t be a return because they are declining to take the gallery cut (usually 50 percent) of whatever sells. Associate Director Carolyn Nickell, 26, designed the catalog, which is printed on a classy paper stock.</p>
<p><strong>Outside jurors</strong></p>
<p>At the opening, all the requisite perks of a commercial gallery are covered. There is a uniformed bartender pouring both red and white wine and issuing little individual bottles of Pellegrino. A table is stocked with grapes and fancy cheese and crackers. For first-timers, they got it right, and also got it right in not presuming to be qualified to select the art. They jobbed that out to a jury of three, consisting of commercial gallery owner Catharine Clark; Julie Lazar, a freelance curator who helped open the Museum of Contemporary Art in Los Angeles; and performance artist Michael Zheng.</p>
<p>The judging was blind to the names of the applicants and their schools. From a pool of 50, eight were selected. UC Berkeley has three artists in the show and Stanford has none. Also shut out were San Francisco State and San Jose State.</p>
<p>Among the artists, school acronyms are flying around the room as if it were an air traffic control tower.</p>
<p>“Before I began the MFA program at CCA, I did a post-doc at SFAI,” says Houtzager, in describing her academic trajectory. Jardini has a master’s degree but not an MFA from SFAI.</p>
<p>As the opening roars on, the crowd holds steady at 50 to 100. The event ends at 8, but people are still there at 8:45. Along the way, gallerists Jardini and Houtzager are asked a mundane question about gallery hours and are momentarily stumped. They shoot each other a “we hadn’t thought of that” look, and then Jardini improvises by saying it’s “by appointment for now.” The hitch is that Sartle.com has moved into the gallery space, eating up the regular weekday hours. Weekend hours for Embark will start in April when the follow-up show, “<em>What Grows Here</em>,” will open.</p>
<p>“We hope to do this,” Houtzager says, “until the end of time.”</p>
<p><em>Sam Whiting is a San Francisco Chronicle staff writer. E-mail: swhiting@sfchronicle.com Twitter: @samwhitingsf</em></p>
<p><strong>Embark: The grand opening exhibition at Embark Gallery runs through March 22 at Building B, Suite 330, Fort Mason Center. Hours are by appointment.</strong></p>

	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->

<article id="post-2108" class="group post-container entry-content">
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">MFA Class 2010 Graduate Beck Suss in a Solo Museum Show</h1>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		
			
		<p>We&#8217;re very proud of  2010 MFA graduate Becky Suss landing a solo show of her work at the Institute of Contemporary Art, University of Philadelphia.</p>
<p><span style="text-decoration: underline">From the Q&amp;A</span>:</p>
<p>Philadelphia artist <a class="inline" href="http://icaphila.org/exhibitions/7466/becky-suss?utm_source=ICA+Mailing+List&amp;utm_campaign=3d2b6f6e24-Program_email_August_24_new_exhibitions_announce&amp;utm_medium=email&amp;utm_term=0_bcfa375d85-3d2b6f6e24-">Becky Suss</a> reimagines her relatives’ mid-century homes through meditative paintings and ceramics. Her canvases memorialize their collected art and objects, opening familial narrative to questions of class, politics, and religion in Cold War America.</p>
<p>As Suss made the final preparations for her first museum solo exhibition, she spoke with <a class="inline" href="http://icaphila.org/staff/3616/becky-huff-hunter">Becky Huff Hunter</a> on her practice. <em><a class="inline" href="http://icaphila.org/exhibitions/7466/becky-suss">Becky Suss</a></em>, organized by Associate Curator <a class="inline" href="http://icaphila.org/staff/1672/kate-kraczon">Kate Kraczon</a>, opens on Wednesday, September 16.</p>
<p><em><strong>ICA</strong>: Your current work is a sort of archaeology of family history and narrative through objects. I love the description of your work as “filtered through the gray zone of memory,” from your</em> Reprefantasion <em>(2013) exhibition at</em> <a href="https://mail.exchange.upenn.edu/owa/redir.aspx?SURL=p0ojOQDbsR4LXvFJVsSTWHM0a3ahGhcNMJYcoES5tOOR7oPTmqzSCGgAdAB0AHAAOgAvAC8AdwB3AHcALgBmAGwAZQBpAHMAaABlAHIALQBvAGwAbABtAGEAbgBnAGEAbABsAGUAcgB5AC4AYwBvAG0ALwBzAGgAbwB3AHMALwAyADAAMQAzAC8AMQAyAF8AcgBlAHAAcgBlAGYAYQBuAHQAYQBzAGkAbwBuAC8A&amp;URL=http%3a%2f%2fwww.fleisher-ollmangallery.com%2fshows%2f2013%2f12_reprefantasion%2f" target="_blank"><em>Fleisher/Ollman</em></a><em>.</em> <em>How do you approach this gray zone as a painter?</em></p>
<p><strong>Becky Suss</strong>: Several years ago I learned about <a href="https://mail.exchange.upenn.edu/owa/redir.aspx?SURL=hwz1riNYUdC0pye5c7sDCBV0zvJSyViNadsIUuN1rMKR7oPTmqzSCGgAdAB0AHAAOgAvAC8AdwB3AHcALgBuAGUAdwB5AG8AcgBrAGUAcgAuAGMAbwBtAC8AbQBhAGcAYQB6AGkAbgBlAC8AMgAwADEANAAvADAANQAvADEAOQAvAHAAYQByAHQAaQBhAGwALQByAGUAYwBhAGwAbAA.&amp;URL=http%3a%2f%2fwww.newyorker.com%2fmagazine%2f2014%2f05%2f19%2fpartial-recall" target="_blank">memory reconsolidation</a>, a relatively new theory that describes the process of what happens when we revisit a memory. It suggests that each time we remember something, the memory is significantly altered, and the changed version takes the place of the original. There are no pristine accounts deep in our brains, only reconsolidated memories containing the traces of all of the other times these memories were recalled.</p>
<p>(<a href="http://icaphila.org/posts/7583/q-a-becky-suss">to continue reading&#8230;</a>)</p>

	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->

</article><!-- #post-2108 -->

<article id="post-2146" class="group post-container entry-content">
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Latest student artist work at the Worth Ryder Art Gallery!</h1>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		
			
		<h3>Here are the news from the interns at the Worth Ryder Art Gallery! This week features student artists, professional artist lecturers and campus art organisations. Take a look!</h3>
<hr />
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p><strong>Student artist interview: Hannah Reinhard </strong>(<em>by Julia Borrebaeck</em>)</p>
<p>Check out this interview with artist Hannah Reinhard. Her latest work is featured at the exhibition <strong>I Know What You Did Last Summer</strong><i> </i>at the Worth Ryder gallery this month. Here, she speaks about what inspired her latest work and why she wanted to become an artist. View interview on the following link: <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Lz2hl-IKM1U">https://www.youtube.com/watch</a></p>
<p><img class="wp-image-2163 aligncenter" src="http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2015/09/Hannah.jpg" alt="Hannah" width="412" height="309" srcset="http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2015/09/Hannah.jpg 960w, http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2015/09/Hannah-300x225.jpg 300w" sizes="(max-width: 412px) 100vw, 412px" /></p>
<hr />
<p><strong style="line-height: 1.5">P</strong><strong style="line-height: 1.5">rofiling Student Artist: Cade Cahalan </strong><span style="line-height: 1.5">(</span><em style="line-height: 1.5">by Kirsten Chen</em><span style="line-height: 1.5">)</span></p>
<p>Cade shoots film photography here at Cal.  His subject matter ranges from his friends to drama landscapes to experimental work. Take a look.</p>
<p><img class="aligncenter wp-image-2149" src="http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2015/09/Kirsten-3-300x218.jpg" alt="Photo by Cade Cahalan" width="380" height="276" srcset="http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2015/09/Kirsten-3-300x218.jpg 300w, http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2015/09/Kirsten-3.jpg 960w" sizes="(max-width: 380px) 100vw, 380px" /></p>
<p><img class="aligncenter wp-image-2150" src="http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2015/09/Kirsten-2-300x216.jpg" alt="Photo by Cade Cahalan" width="380" height="273" srcset="http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2015/09/Kirsten-2-300x216.jpg 300w, http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2015/09/Kirsten-2-1024x737.jpg 1024w" sizes="(max-width: 380px) 100vw, 380px" /></p>
<p><span style="font-weight: 400"><em><img class="aligncenter wp-image-2151" src="http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2015/09/Kirsten-1-300x199.jpg" alt="" width="380" height="252" /></em></span></p>
<p><span style="font-weight: 400"><em>Check out more of his work at <a href="http://cade-cahalan.com/" target="_blank">http://cade-cahalan.com/</a></em></span></p>
<hr />
<p><strong>Q&amp;A with Student Artist Chrissy Curl from IKWYDLS </strong>(<em>by Lina Nguyen</em>)</p>
<p><em><span style="font-weight: 400">If you could only give one advice about studying abroad, what would it be?<br />
</span></em><span style="font-weight: 400">“Keep an open mind to what daily life will be like — you&#8217;ll definitely have lots of fun, but you might also experience unexpected downturns.”</span></p>
<p>Chrissy Curl is a senior double-majoring in Art Practice and Theater &amp; Performance Studies. Her piece, <em>Crispy Others</em>, talks about dispelling the common notion that studying abroad is simply an &#8220;idyllic time full of adventure and exploration and short on studying and responsibility.&#8221; &#8220;Crispy Others&#8221; is currently being showcased alongside other student artist works in the <strong>I Know What You Did Last Summer</strong> exhibition in the <strong>Worth Ryder Art Gallery</strong>.</p>
<p><span style="font-weight: 400">Chrissy says she would highly recommend studying abroad for those who have the opportunity to do so. For more information about studying abroad, check out the fair happening this Friday, September 25th, 2015!<img class="wp-image-2155 aligncenter" src="http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2015/09/ChrissyCurl-300x200.jpg" alt="ChrissyCurl" width="470" height="313" srcset="http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2015/09/ChrissyCurl-300x200.jpg 300w, http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2015/09/ChrissyCurl-1024x681.jpg 1024w, http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2015/09/ChrissyCurl.jpg 1200w" sizes="(max-width: 470px) 100vw, 470px" /> </span></p>
<p><span style="font-weight: 400"><em>Get more information on studying abroad at</em> <em><a href="http://studyabroadfair.berkeley.edu/">http://studyabroadfair.berkeley.edu/</a></em></span></p>
<hr />
<p><strong>UC Berkeley Art Community ‘Outlet’ Co-presidents: Allison Zhong &amp; Sophie Li</strong> (<em>by Sosun Park</em>)</p>
<p><span style="font-weight: 400">Outlet is a student art organization that seeks to provide a creative outlet and access to supplies for visual artists of all experience levels. They work to serve the UC Berkeley campus and surrounding community by providing open access to arts education. Check out one of<br />
Outlet&#8217;s weekly studio meetings on Mondays at 7pm in 172 Wurster or Thursdays at 7pm in 89 Dwinelle! Email <a  href="javascript:smae_decode('b3V0bGV0LmJlcmtlbGV5QGdtYWlsLmNvbQ==');" >&#111;&#117;&#116;&#108;&#101;&#116;&#046;&#098;&#101;&#114;&#107;&#101;&#108;&#101;&#121;&#064;&#103;&#109;&#097;&#105;&#108;&#046;&#099;&#111;&#109;</a> with inquiries.</span> <a href="http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2015/09/Sosun-Outlet-Banner-Design.jpg"><br />
</a></p>
<p><em><span style="font-weight: 400"><img class="aligncenter wp-image-2156" src="http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2015/09/Sosun-Outlet-Banner-Design-300x113.jpg" alt="" width="380" height="143" srcset="http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2015/09/Sosun-Outlet-Banner-Design-300x113.jpg 300w, http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2015/09/Sosun-Outlet-Banner-Design-1024x384.jpg 1024w, http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2015/09/Sosun-Outlet-Banner-Design.jpg 1280w" sizes="(max-width: 380px) 100vw, 380px" />More information and images at </span><a href="https://outletberkeley.wordpress.com"><span style="font-weight: 400">https://outletberkeley.wordpress.com</span></a></em></p>
<hr />
<p><strong>Artist Talk w/ Philip Ross </strong>(<em>by Celia Chu</em>)<span style="font-weight: 400"><br />
<em>Monday September 21, 2015 at 7:30 pm &#8211; 9 pm. </em></span></p>
<p><span style="font-weight: 400">“Use what you have, to move yourself forward” &#8211; Philip Ross </span></p>
<p><span style="font-weight: 400">This artist talk was not only enlightening from the art and scientific research that Philip Ross has created over a span of a decade, there was an emotional quality that put emphasis on his own feelings and approach toward his studies on mushroom/fungus/mycelium. He approached his love for art in biology with the projects within a time-basis as well as a lot of experimentation. His projects are wide variations on growth, and these projects gravitate from his fascination with a philosophical approach toward relations between humans and technology. </span></p>
<p><span style="font-weight: 400">His quote really spoke to me as an artist and this quote not only pertains within the category of Art, but as a specific threshold that I can also relate to with my art works. The amount of determination it takes to create such masterpieces, helped him prosper as an artist. </span></p>
<p><span style="font-weight: 400">He made a point in his Q&amp;A, and from what I interpreted from him mentioning, “understanding the mono-biological approach, and not just [the] mechanical” is indeed an ‘organic’ approach toward learning through experimentation and not everything (as well as his projects) has to be completed or approached through a regulated system or by a set of rules/instruction.</span><br />
<span style="font-weight: 400">I hope many others were thrilled to hear from Ross’ artist talk and to have gained something special from his kind words. </span></p>
<p><em><span style="font-weight: 400">More information / video: </span><a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=GRnJeNurdEY"><span style="font-weight: 400">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=GRnJeNurdEY</span></a></em></p>
<hr />
<p><strong>Student artist interview with Jinoh Ryu</strong> (<em>by Hyesun Kim</em>)</p>
<p><em><span style="font-weight: 400">1. How important is your choice of medium in conveying your thoughts and intentions for the piece? Does it have any relationship to the current digital age?</span></em><span style="font-weight: 400"><br />
</span><span style="font-weight: 400">I could have taken a high-quality photo of myself applying peanut butter on my face. But I wanted to take advantage of the gloomy screen quality of the PhotoBooth program. What I ended up conveying is a lack of clarity, which raises anxiety in this era of meanings accessed immediately. Even to this day, I cannot pin down what I intended to explicate in the video. So the video has a masturbatory element; something perturbed me, but I was too tired to dig into what it was. I just had to get it out of my way.</span><span style="font-weight: 400"><br />
</span><span style="font-weight: 400"> </span><span style="font-weight: 400"><br />
</span><em><span style="font-weight: 400">2. What does peanut butter mean to you?</span><span style="font-weight: 400"><br />
</span></em><span style="font-weight: 400">My scatological engagement with peanut butter is an act of resilience. In my video, the shitty peanut butter that I luxuriated in boasts 24% trans-fat. Essentially, the medical industry is stating, “Never put anything that is ‘trans’ in your body.” Yet as a transgender person, I welcome and embrace anything that is “trans.” And peanut butter mitigates the dull taste of other food such as celery, also known as the bane of my existence. But peanut butter never told me what it means to me. To peanut butter, I can say only that I am a better platform than those ugly bagels from the Golden Bear Café in capturing its titillating aesthetic.</span><span style="font-weight: 400"><br />
</span><span style="font-weight: 400"> </span><span style="font-weight: 400"><br />
</span><em><span style="font-weight: 400">3. You described your artwork to be therapeutic. How so? Why?</span><span style="font-weight: 400"><br />
</span></em><span style="font-weight: 400">Although the gallery presents my video on mute, I have background music titled “Zero Gravity” by Korean rap star Zion.T. As I was listening to that lullaby-like music, I made an impromptu video which became my original submission. After clicking the “Finish Record” button, I had a gut sense that Deleuze and Guattari who coined the term ‘faciality’ communicated to me through orgasmic intensity. And what is more therapeutic than a scholarly orgasm? Right?</span></p>
<hr />
<p><strong>S</strong><strong>tudent artist interview with Anthony Roberts</strong> (<em>by Taylor R. McAllister</em>)</p>
<p>Fallen Leaf No. 1, 2, and 3 from Series: <i>Place</i>. 2015 photographs on aluminum panels. Series of 3 at 20&#8243; x 30&#8243; each: 20&#8243; x 94&#8243; total</p>
<p>Anthony Roberts artist&#8217;s statement for his triptych in <strong>I Know What You Did Last Summer</strong> gallery show; <em>“</em>I often find myself identifying with the feeling I get in a place rather than the blinking blue dot on my phone that says “you are here.”</p>
<p><i>Place</i> is about approaching places in this world as unique individuals. Through the abstraction of the physical landscape, my goal is to reveal a ‘feeling of being’ in each of those places. While removing any obvious geographical associations, these photographs are ultimately landscapes and I make no attempt to hide this. Instead, I want the viewer to experience the feeling and the sense of place in these photographs without the need to identify their exact location on a map and ultimately their physicality in society.</p>
<p>Much of this project has been shot while travelling along the California coast, in the mountains and on the roads in between.</p>
<p>All of the photos in this series are created in-camera through a photographic technique I call ‘dragging the shutter’ and are not manipulated during post processing.</p>
<p>&#8220;The exploration of individuality in contemporary society is a driving force in my artwork. Presently, I’m working on three separate photo projects and one mixed media project addressing this theme from different perspectives.”   – Anthony Roberts</p>
<p><em>How would you introduce yourself as an individual and an artist?</em></p>
<p>&#8220;Introductions are hard, aren&#8217;t they? Especially when they are meant to really introduce. I think it&#8217;s challenging to distill oneself into a short and hopefully articulate description but I&#8217;ll do my best for you. I see myself as older than some and younger than others, but young at heart either way. I&#8217;m a bit of a wanderer, both physically and spiritually. I think I try to use that spirit in my artwork &#8211; I mean, I think my artwork has a bit of a wandering spirit in itself as well. I mostly focus on photography, lately, but I consider myself an artist in general.&#8221;</p>
<p><em>Could you tell me a short history of how you got here?</em></p>
<p>&#8220;A short history of nearly everything and mostly nothing&#8221; by A. Roberts. Born in Queens, New York in 1979 I just snuck in as a 70&#8217;s child. Total product of the 80&#8217;s, particular the movies. I still love 80&#8217;s movies and 80&#8217;s movie soundtracks. I could talk about that (and often do) for way longer than I should. Maybe one day I&#8217;ll make some artwork that directly pulls from that genre. I think that could be really cool. My grandmother still has an ever-yellowing paper drawing of an airplane that I allegedly made at the age of two, admittedly I do not remember drawing it. I actually included a wordier version of that grandma-drawing anecdote in my statement for my Berkeley application. I guess they liked it. I moved to California in 2001 and have considered the bay area home ever since then. I guess I&#8217;ve always been an &#8220;artist&#8221; but I never really accepted that as a possible &#8220;career&#8221; / &#8220;life&#8221; track until a couple of years ago while I was ski-bumming in Jackson Hole with my girlfriend. We had just started dating and decided to go on an adventure. After a year and a half of basically just playing in the mountains we were ready to come back to the bay area and I was ready to come back to school and finish my degree. I came back to school for architecture, then started to feel like an old man and after some great classes at the College of Marin with a couple of great art instructors, I switched into art. And here I am.</p>
<p><em>What is the driving force behind your work?</em></p>
<p>&#8220;I think the real driving force behind my work is that sense of wandering I mentioned earlier. I&#8217;m constantly searching. I&#8217;m also really interested in exploring the role of individuality in contemporary society, from various perspectives. I actually think these landscapes are part of that exploration of individuality. In the case of this series, I&#8217;m exploring the individuality of place and then how we experience that place as a society and also as individuals.&#8221;</p>
<p><em>“Fallen Leaf No. 1-3” are all photographs. What other media do you work in, if any?</em></p>
<p>&#8220;In the last couple of years I&#8217;ve completely fallen in love with photography, so it&#8217;s definitely my medium of choice. That being said, I also paint and draw. I&#8217;m currently exploring painting on large printed photographs. I&#8217;ve dabbled in some sculpture. Carved my first large stone last year. And I love working with wood. There&#8217;s something about the smell and the feel of a wood shop, or even the part of the apartment I&#8217;ve taken over as a makeshift wood shop. Oh yeah, and I like building snowmen, does that count?&#8221;</p>
<p><em>Tell us a bit about the physical process of creating this triptych.</em></p>
<p>&#8220;The process is really pretty straight forward. I always have a camera with me. When I first got my camera a few years I was so nervous to carry it around with me all the time, but now it&#8217;s a constant companion. The short version is really short. I find myself in a beautiful place, I take the photo and then later I process it into a jpeg and print it. I take pride in the fact that I generally don&#8217;t manipulate these images in Photoshop.&#8221;</p>
<p><em>In your artist’s statement you called the technique you used, “dragging the shutter” How did you develop this?</em></p>
<p>&#8220;This series of abstract landscapes are the result of traveling to beautiful places. I wanted to record the feeling of being in those places but I never wanted to be a landscape photographer or even shoot landscapes. About two years ago, I was in Yosemite and obviously I had to shoot that place. It&#8217;s just so beautiful. I was taking a time lapse of the moon rising over half dome and then I got the urge to grab the camera and sweep it across the landscape with a long exposure. The resulting image was really ethereal and kind of abstract and very pink. I thought, hmmm. This is kind of cool. Since then I&#8217;ve been working on this process of long exposures and camera movement to create these abstract landscapes. It&#8217;s still evolving but I like the directions it&#8217;s headed.&#8221;</p>
<p><em>What was your thought process when developing the work?</em></p>
<p>&#8220;There really wasn&#8217;t much more to it, at first, than just wanting to capture the feeling of a place, without capturing it&#8217;s geographically exactness. I think I mention the &#8220;feeling of being&#8221; of a place in my artist statement. That&#8217;s really it. I want to figure out ways to create images that evoke the feeling of a place without the need to attach oneself to its exact physical location on a map.&#8221;</p>
<p><em>As a series, how do you see Places developing?</em></p>
<p>&#8220;I think it&#8217;s one of those projects that will just continue and continue, until maybe I get bored with it one day. For now it&#8217;s a great project for me because it allows me to travel, really, it inspires and even forces me to travel, in order to create these images and that&#8217;s great. I would really like to travel more. It&#8217;s also great to experiment with the camera as a tool for creating artwork, not just &#8220;capturing&#8221; images.&#8221;</p>
<p><em>Where does “Fallen Leaf No. 1-3” lie in the scope of your Places series? (Beginning, middle, end?)</em></p>
<p>&#8220;I&#8217;d say I&#8217;m still in the beginning stages of the photo project and maybe in the middle stages of my life, so somewhere in that zone&#8230;&#8221;</p>
<p><em>For more information about his work visit <a href="http://www.arobertsphotography.com/">http://www.arobertsphotography.com/</a></em></p>

	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->

</article><!-- #post-2146 -->

<article id="post-2228" class="group post-container entry-content">
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Bay Area Artists at the Worth Ryder Art Gallery</h1>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		
			
		<h3>Scroll to see what&#8217;s up at the Worth Ryder Art Gallery! The interns talk about the bay area artists in this weeks exhibition; <em>(processing) – Bay Area Artists and the Archive</em></h3>
<hr />
<p><span style="font-weight: 400"><strong>Randy Hussong&#8217;s show </strong>(<em>by Sosun Park</em>)</span></p>
<p>Randy Hussong is faculty in Art Practice department. He teaches printmaking and sculpture.<br />
He is well-known to a post-modern, late minimal, neo-dada, pop conceptualist folk artist etc.<br />
<strong>Come to his special show! The Berkeley Arts Festival, 2133 University Ave, Berkeley CA, September 15th – Halloween</strong></p>
<p><span style="font-weight: 400">“Randy Hussong &#8211; Old Boots/ New Work</span></p>
<p><span style="font-weight: 400">Sax blown, the penguin slipped on the freshly melted ice cap his friend the polar bear had recently bemoaned, and half-way around the world hence, round the gulf, there whirled a brass Dixieland bass line, boom-boom-boom. Up in New York, a rocketing pianist, sophomore at Johnson in her reflection. Overseas again, having defected, a great white menace blows the whistle, a Silent Gesture screaming across the sky, over Net Privacy. Herein lies the modern gorilla war. But still there exists a guerrilla 1%, capitalizing The Sonorous Buddha in Las Vegas colors. Put on your bright warm boots, step in, step up, tune in, or simply drop in.” &#8211; Randy Hussong<br />
</span></p>
<p><span style="font-weight: 400"><img class="wp-image-2233 aligncenter" src="http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2015/10/Randy-Hussong.jpg" alt="Randy Hussong" width="418" height="1013" /></span></p>
<hr />
<p><span style="font-weight: 400"><strong>Interview with Danielle Schlunegger </strong><em>(by Hyesun Kim)</em></span></p>
<p><span style="font-weight: 400"><em><img class="wp-image-2229 alignright" src="http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2015/10/DanielleSchluneggerinterviewwithHyesun-Kim-e1445384361636.jpg" alt="DanielleSchluneggerinterviewwithHyesun Kim" width="255" height="346" /></em></span></p>
<p><em style="line-height: 1.5">What qualities of a tent enhances the overall experience of your work (specifically, the current work at Worth Ryder)? How does the dynamics of the gallery space interact with your work? Does it bring the viewers inside or outside?<br />
</em><span style="font-weight: 400"><br />
</span><span style="font-weight: 400">Walking into the gallery and immediately being confronted with the structure of the tent is intended to be a little jarring and overwhelming in a way that tries to inspire a feeling of curiosity- of wanting to know more. I am hoping to light that spark in people that causes them to feel a great desire to investigate, similar to how an explorer of the 19th century must have felt coming upon something so entirely unexpected. This allows the viewer to relate in a small way to Marcus Kelli. The tent creates its own environment, and the shadows of leaves on the tent are reminiscent of being outdoors and far from the inside of a gallery. The installation is meant to be immersive and being able to touch and hold objects helps to make the viewer slow down and feel transported to another place and time within the gallery.</span></p>
<p><em><span style="font-weight: 400">What do you want viewers to take away from your work? </span></em></p>
<p><span style="font-weight: 400">I hope to be able to awake curiosity in people, so that they walk away with a renewed sense of wonder about the natural world. For a modern person overloaded with technological responsibilities, connecting with nature can become a welcomed source of meditation and deep thinking. As our society shifts, we have reached a point where humans must make crucial decisions about the future of our planet. It is my hope that Marcus Kelli’s story can be used as a springboard for self-reflection on our relationship with nature and how we continue to shape it.</span></p>
<hr />
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p><span style="font-weight: 400"><strong>Describing the work of Heather Murphy in the current exhibition </strong><em>(by Julia Borrebaeck)</em></span></p>
<p><span style="font-weight: 400">In this month’s exhibition, Heather Murphy constructed a highly modern take on how people are constantly bombarded with information on the Internet. She made three short videos filled with devastating news mixed with entertainment clips, depicting the sharp contrast between the two most common types of news. Besides her way of portraying this, through using characteristics from popular digital platforms such as tweets from twitter, she has also chosen an interesting medium. You have to connect your smartphone to one of three video specific Wi-Fi networks and then open the safari app to view the video. On display at the exhibition is essentially three routers, headphones and instructions how to access the videos. Just as the phenomenon portrayed in the videos, the videos themselves are “only virtually present in the space”. These two images shows the contrast between the entertainment icon Taylor Swift and an extremist environment.</span></p>
<p><span style="font-weight: 400"><img class="alignnone wp-image-2232 size-medium" src="http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2015/10/IMG_0902-300x169.png" alt="IMG_0902" width="300" height="169" srcset="http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2015/10/IMG_0902-300x169.png 300w, http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2015/10/IMG_0902-1024x577.png 1024w, http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2015/10/IMG_0902.png 1136w" sizes="(max-width: 300px) 100vw, 300px" /><img class="alignnone wp-image-2231 size-medium" src="http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2015/10/IMG_0900-300x169.png" alt="IMG_0900" width="300" height="169" srcset="http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2015/10/IMG_0900-300x169.png 300w, http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2015/10/IMG_0900-1024x577.png 1024w, http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2015/10/IMG_0900.png 1136w" sizes="(max-width: 300px) 100vw, 300px" /></span></p>
<hr />
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p><strong>Experiencing “Kalapani” (artist Andrew Ananda Voogel) </strong><em>(by Taylor R. McAllister)</em></p>
<p><span style="font-weight: 400">In the back of the Worth Ryder Art Gallery are two tall onyx curtains as doors to another space. Curious viewers will not be deterred by what immediately follows them: a black room, so dark that one cannot see their hand in front of their face. “There has to be more”, is what runs through the minds of most viewers. And there is. Curiosity gets you in, but patience gives you the art piece. By thirty seconds of standing hyperaware in the dark, flickers appear on a previously indistinguishable opposite wall. At two minutes, the viewer is hypnotized by the rhythmic movements of a dark film that is barely there. Gentle pale white bands of light flow back and forth; with one’s eyes adjusted they can see the subtle details of waves rolling over the dark land, meeting other waves, and a rock in the ocean standing against them. The mood of “Kalapani” for me was one of mild unease and uncertainty, as we do not usually stand in such darkness. And even with one’s eyes adjusted to the film and space, it is still impossible to see what is further out at sea. It is a great wide unknown far more mysterious than when seen in daylight. There is a mild sense of being at the edge of a precipice. And yet, as I watched the movement of the waves was a visual lullaby of light that balanced that feeling. The repetitive push-pull motions maintained the well-known sense of breathing that the ocean gives, despite the darkness. In its own way, the video was both unsettling and meditative. And as contrary as it seems, these two opposites stem from the same elements of the piece: the darkness and the vastness of the ocean.  Andrew Voogel’s work is a beautiful experience, and quite a view.</span></p>
<p><span style="font-weight: 400">Text edit: </span><span style="font-weight: 400">And yet as I watched the movement, the waves were a visual lullaby of light that balanced that feeling. </span></p>
<hr />
<p><strong>Soey Milk’s “PIDA” in Hashimoto Contemporary</strong> <em>(by Celia Chu)</em></p>
<p><span style="font-weight: 400">Soey Milk is one of my favorite Los Angeles artists that I first discovered through her Instagram profile, </span><a href="https://instagram.com/soeymilky/"><span style="font-weight: 400">@soeymilky</span></a><span style="font-weight: 400">. Her art speaks of self empowerment and exploration of the human body. Her mesmerizing perfection of detail line work and pressure<br />
of the utensils to capture wide variations ofgradients. There is an elegance to her distinct yet abstract line work that plays well with the meaning behind each and every defined graphite pieces with the incorporation of oils. </span></p>
<p><span style="font-weight: 400">I wanted to solely focus on her as a blossoming artist, as well as her most recent solo artist show, “PIDA” in Hashimoto Contemporary. PIDA is still open to the public by this Saturday, October 24, 2015. PIDA is an expression of growth, and more precisely &#8211; a blossoming effect. I personally see Soey Milk as a growing artist, and she captivates her audience with fine fabrics and mesh materials in her realistic pieces. </span></p>
<p><a href="http://www.spoke-art.com/2013/09/12/1201/"><i><span style="font-weight: 400">Cirrus</span></i></a><span style="font-weight: 400"> is a mystical piece I encountered and is also being sold at the gallery space. The curvaceous depiction of a woman is filled with emotion from border-to-border all the way to the fine line details of the speckled hair on her head to her pointed toes. The essence of deep reds draping her torso to the slight touches of peachy pinks on the soles of her feet define the signature color palette of what reds signify; blooming. The remnants of color surrounding this whimsy piece is romantic and touches on a sense of renewal. The abstraction in the background does not take away from the piece, it only enhances the subject of this woman.<br />
</span></p>
<p><span style="font-weight: 400"><img class="wp-image-2234 aligncenter" src="http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2015/10/SoeyMilk_Cirrus.jpg" alt="SoeyMilk_Cirrus" width="367" height="517" /> </span></p>
<p><span style="font-weight: 400">For more information about her work and talent, click </span><a href="http://milkbomb.blogspot.com/"><span style="font-weight: 400">here</span></a><span style="font-weight: 400"> for her website. </span></p>

	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->

<article id="post-2509" class="group post-container entry-content">
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">New Reviews and Interviews with Art Practice Undergrads</h1>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		
			
		<p><em>The Worth Ryder Art Gallery Publicity Interns regularly review exhibitions, interview undergrads, and write about artwork for our News page.</em></p>
<h3><em><img class="alignnone wp-image-2511" src="http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2016/03/RevillaKatie2.jpg" alt="RevillaKatie2" width="300" height="304" srcset="http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2016/03/RevillaKatie2.jpg 518w, http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2016/03/RevillaKatie2-296x300.jpg 296w" sizes="(max-width: 300px) 100vw, 300px" /> <img class="alignnone wp-image-2512" src="http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2016/03/RevillaKatie1.jpg" alt="RevillaKatie1" width="300" height="373" srcset="http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2016/03/RevillaKatie1.jpg 422w, http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2016/03/RevillaKatie1-241x300.jpg 241w" sizes="(max-width: 300px) 100vw, 300px" />   </em></h3>
<h3>Gordon Hall &#8211; &#8220;Speculative Lecture&#8221;</h3>
<h3>Reviewed by Katie Revilla</h3>
<p><strong>Gordon Hall</strong>, a performance artist, writer, and founder of the <a href="http://experimentallectures.org/">Center for Experimental Lectures</a> recently came to give a propositional and improvisational lecture at UC Berkeley. For me the lecture redefined and expanded what can be considered an artist&#8217;s talk. Two figures were positioned within the space, completely still and motionless. Hall, who was in the corner of the room perched upon a pedestal, began gestures and movement, slowly moving themselves in different variations. As they announced act 1, scene 1, phase 1, they got up from the pedestal and began to move and interact with two large wooden parentheses. As both Gordon and the other performer moved throughout the space with the large wooden parentheses, the conversation began.</p>
<p>Hall read aloud twice in the performance, as well as from their phone, with imagery that to me felt like recalling a memory. The words were colors, objects, items, and feelings all of which in sighted something in me that created certain feelings of nostalgia. Although I don&#8217;t completely understand the relationship between the performance and the recalling of these writings, after Hall repeated it, I tried to imagine and feel what they were describing for the rest of the piece. Parts of the performance were also with the lights off, which I found to be a really sensory experience. One could see shadows occasionally, you could hear them moving, and as a performer moved close to where you were sitting, you felt their bodily presence.</p>
<p>Overall, this piece has so many different layers and complexities within itself. I loved how this piece confronted language and challenged how we perceive it. It made me think a lot more about the body and how we can transmit such an array of information. Also, coming from a background and understanding that artist&#8217;s lectures are where the artist talks at the audience and then might do a question and answer period at the end of the talk, this piece was able to challenge and shift the ways that artists can provide information through creating a new conversation. Artists don&#8217;t necessarily have to do a report on their work but instead convey the meaning of their work through a performance such as this. I found Gordon inspiring, and I&#8217;m excited to follow their work as it progresses in the future.</p>
<p style="text-align: center">* * *</p>
<h3>Video Interview with Shari Paladino from MFA First Year Exhibition</h3>
<h3>by Shannon Kim</h3>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p style="text-align: center"> * * *</p>
<h3>Artist Erin Kim</h3>
<h3>Interviewed by Sae Bom Choi</h3>
<p><img class="alignnone wp-image-2514" src="http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2016/03/ChoiSaebom2-300x300.jpg" alt="ChoiSaebom2" width="400" height="400" srcset="http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2016/03/ChoiSaebom2-300x300.jpg 300w, http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2016/03/ChoiSaebom2-150x150.jpg 150w, http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2016/03/ChoiSaebom2.jpg 640w" sizes="(max-width: 400px) 100vw, 400px" /></p>
<p><strong>Tell me about yourself.</strong></p>
<p>My name is Erin Kim and I am a junior majoring in Landscape Architecture. I have been interested in art and design since I was in elementary school. Ever since I became passionate about art, I enjoyed going to art galleries and museums to get inspirations, and explore new ideas to motivate myself.</p>
<p><strong>What work do you most enjoying doing?</strong></p>
<p>I enjoy working with a variety of medium including acrylic, oil paint, ink, colored pencils, graphite, and more. I like to combine different types of medium to create more interesting effects.</p>
<p><img class="alignnone wp-image-2515" src="http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2016/03/ChoiSaebom-300x202.jpg" alt="ChoiSaebom" width="400" height="269" srcset="http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2016/03/ChoiSaebom-300x202.jpg 300w, http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2016/03/ChoiSaebom-768x517.jpg 768w, http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2016/03/ChoiSaebom.jpg 951w" sizes="(max-width: 400px) 100vw, 400px" /></p>
<p><strong>What themes do you pursue?</strong></p>
<p>I don&#8217;t really have a theme that I want to pursue through my works but I try to create works that are meaningful to me. The process that I go through before starting an art piece is that I go over all the photo albums and try to choose a moment or subject that I want to make special by adding aesthetic view to the specific subject.</p>
<p><strong>What inspired you to major in landscape and minor in art?</strong></p>
<p>I was undeclared in College of Letters and Science until the second semester of my sophomore year. The major factor that motivated me to transfer to College of Environmental Design was taking an Environmental Design (ED) class second semester of freshman year. Although I knew that I was interested in art and design, I was uncertain about what I wanted to major in. I had a lot of fun in ED class; the class was about problem solving projects using art and design knowledge. Then I looked more into the Landscape Architecture department and took some Landscape classes then declared as Landscape architecture major. I also wanted to pursue my artistic view by minoring in art.</p>
<p><img class="alignnone wp-image-2513" src="http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2016/03/ChoiSaebom3-300x300.png" alt="ChoiSaebom3" width="400" height="400" srcset="http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2016/03/ChoiSaebom3-300x300.png 300w, http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2016/03/ChoiSaebom3-150x150.png 150w, http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2016/03/ChoiSaebom3-768x768.png 768w, http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2016/03/ChoiSaebom3-1024x1024.png 1024w" sizes="(max-width: 400px) 100vw, 400px" /></p>
<p><strong>How much drawing do you have to go through to prepare for a piece?</strong></p>
<p>I spend a lot of time coming up with an idea for an art piece. I do some quick sketches of all the ideas I have and narrow down to what I want specifically.</p>
<p><strong>What is your dream project? What’s your goal?</strong></p>
<p>My goal is to continue creating art works that are meaningful to me. I always feel that photography is a temporary snapshot of memories. But creating an artwork of the special moment can last longer and remain more valuable to me. My dream project is to create a series of special moments of four years in college.</p>
<p style="text-align: center">* * *</p>
<h3> <img class="alignnone wp-image-2516" src="http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2016/03/ono-yes.jpg" alt="ono - yes" width="400" height="360" srcset="http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2016/03/ono-yes.jpg 637w, http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2016/03/ono-yes-300x270.jpg 300w" sizes="(max-width: 400px) 100vw, 400px" /></h3>
<h3>Critic’s Perspective: Rediscovering Yoko Ono</h3>
<h3>by Michele Meltzer</h3>
<p style="text-align: left">Professor Scott Saul from our neighboring English Department at UC Berkeley has a new podcast show, <em>Chapter &amp; Verse</em>, which features discursive conversations between himself and other academics/critics of media culture. In this episode, Professor Saul brings on Professor Julia Bryan-Wilson of the UC Berkeley History of Art Department to discuss the art of Yoko Ono.</p>
<p>Critical attention to Ono’s work and its centrality within a number of artistic movements, especially her creative output through the 1960s, was for many years lost to an amnesiac cultural narrative which has long held Ono merely as the collaborator, muse, and widow of pop legend John Lennon. Her work—ranging widely from performance art, to instructional pieces, to video and beyond—and its importance to the history of contemporary art have recently regained recognition through exhibitions such as MoMa’s “<em>Yoko Ono: One Woman Show, 1960-1971</em>”. Here, Professor Saul along with Professor Bryan-Wilson honor this story which regards Ono as an artist first and foremost, helping audiences recall why Ono’s work stands on its own. The talk involves a rich examination of Ono’s personal story, its influence on her ever-evolving creative process, and the formal risks present in Ono’s art which warrant its acclaim.</p>
<p style="text-align: center">* * *</p>
<h3>First Friday and Community</h3>
<h3>by Hannah Laher</h3>
<p>We all choose to be an art major, and an art major specifically in Berkeley.  As a soon to be graduate of this program, and a local of the Bay Area since I was a child- the thoughts of what can this community do for me has come to mind more often than once.  With the tech-industry boom, San Francisco and Oakland have been in the spotlight a lot these days, but do people on a creative career have a place within this spotlight?</p>
<p>Through my research I have found that artist are this community here in the Bay Area, which is why I want to remind everyone about Oaklands First Fridays, starting up again on March 4th.  With their vision statement of “striv[ing] to be the premier Oakland First Fridays Event as the epicenter for the community to come together to celebrate, sustain &amp; support the arts,”  this event has brought everyone together within the Oakland and the arts community.  This tradition started in 2006, with the Art Murmur events, which has now transformed in the this esteemed event for all to enjoy.</p>
<p>According to a report written by Victoria Swift, “Economics Impact of the Oakland First Fridays”, this event is supporting more than just the arts communities.  The influx of people coming in has had an amazing impact on the local businesses, some owners stating a 100 to 250 percent increase in profits on event nights.</p>
<p>We are in a great place for our art.  We just need to help support the community we are going to a part of because the horizon of graduation comes quicker than you expect.</p>
<p>With your free AC bus transit card, take the 1 route and walk a few blocks for the First Friday event starting this month.</p>

	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->

	 			<h1><a href="http://art.berkeley.edu">  AP Undergrad Ramon De Santiago is now a Haas Scholar!</a></h1>

<article id="post-2556" class="group post-container entry-content">
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">AP Undergrad Ramon De Santiago is now a Haas Scholar!</h1>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		
			
		<p>Art Practice undergraduate student Ramon De Santiago has been selected as a Haas Scholar for the 2016-2017 academic year. They had the largest number of applicants ever in the history of the program this year, so the department if very proud of  Ramon&#8217;s accomplishment.</p>
<p>What makes a Haas Scholar? Well, they are:</p>
<p><em>*Academically Talented: value learning both for its own sake and to make a difference in the world</em><br />
<em>*Community-Oriented:  desire to be part of an interdisciplinary academic community by learning from one&#8217;s peers </em><br />
<em>*Future Leaders:  ready to take on the challenge of conducting an independent research or creative project that will culminate in a senior/honors thesis</em></p>
<p>What does a Haas Scholar receive? Well:</p>
<p><em>*One-on-one mentoring from members of the UC-Berkeley faculty</em><br />
<em> *A seminar in the Fall (1 units, P/NP) and a writing workshop in the Spring to assist with the research and writing process</em><br />
<em> *An opportunity to present the results of your work at a professional academic conference in January</em><br />
<em> *Assistance with graduate school and fellowship applications, career counseling, connections with almost 400 Haas Scholars alumni, and other support to help you realize your individual post-graduation plans </em><br />
<em> *Membership in an alumni community (nearly 400 strong and growing each year!) that provides ongoing professional development and support</em><br />
<em> *Financial support during their fellowship year which includes a summer living stipend, a semester/academic year living stipend, research expenses, and a completion stipend</em></p>
<p>This is a <strong>huge</strong> accomplishment, and so a big shout-out to Ramon!</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>

	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->

</article><!-- #post-2556 -->

<article id="post-2681" class="group post-container entry-content">
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Art Practice has awarded José Figueroa (MFA’16) a Sam Francis Fellowship to participate in the 2016-2017 Grad Fellowship Program at the Headlands Center for the Arts.</h1>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		
			
		<p>From the artist:</p>
<p><em>&#8220;My work systematically chronicles different layers of my identity, with the goal of deconstructing basic epistemological categories that separate the aesthetic from the everyday, the sacred from the profane. I look at the repetitive gestures embedded in folklore, religion, and ideology through various means of expression from painting, ceramics, photography, video, and performance to interviews, collaborations, texts, relics, and ready-mades. I position myself as a shaman who seeks to unveil the masks of history. My goal is to create transformative healing experiences for my viewers, surroundings, and former selves.&#8221;</em></p>
<p>For more information on Jose Figueroa and the Headlands Center for the Arts: <a href="http://www.headlands.org/artist/jose-figueroa/">http://www.headlands.org/artist/jose-figueroa/</a></p>

	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->

</article><!-- #post-2681 -->

<article id="post-2707" class="group post-container entry-content">
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Exhibition at Slide Space 123, featuring Art Practice Grad Student Nicki Green: &#8220;Intro (Writing&#8217;s on the Wall)&#8221;</h1>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		
			
		<p>&#8220;We are not simply one demographic or another. The different descriptors framing us may in part describe us, but the pictures they draw are incomplete. For some they become qualifiers making one a subset of the whole, addendums that keeps one from being referred to as what we desire to be&#8230;&#8221; (<a href="http://www.slidespace123.org/writing-on-the-wall">more</a>)</p>
<p><em>Slide Space</em> is an exciting new exhibition space at Mills College.</p>
<p><strong>Opening : </strong>Wednesday, September 14, 6:00 &#8211; 8:00 p.m.</p>
<p><em>Exhibition curated by our co-director Jackie Im</em></p>

	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->

</article><!-- #post-2707 -->

			
		
		</div><!-- #content-container -->


	</div><!-- #main -->

	<footer id="footer" class="wideness" role="contentinfo">


		<div id="footer-content">
			
			<div class="footer-nav">

<article id="post-2882" class="group post-container entry-content">
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Artist Mildred Howard to be the Commencement Speaker at the 2017 Art Practice Graduation.</h1>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		
			
		<p>The Department of Art Practice is pleased to announce that our 2017 Commencement Speaker will be Bay Area artist <strong>Mildred Howard</strong>. Commencement will be on Monday, May 15th at Hertz Hall .</p>
<div id="attachment_2889" style="width: 690px" class="wp-caption alignnone"><img class="wp-image-2889 size-full" src="http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2017/03/Mildrew-Howard.jpg" alt="" width="680" height="503" srcset="http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2017/03/Mildrew-Howard.jpg 680w, http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2017/03/Mildrew-Howard-300x222.jpg 300w" sizes="(max-width: 680px) 100vw, 680px" /><p class="wp-caption-text">Mildred Howard, &#8220;Laila Ali&#8221;, 2010. Mixed Media Assemblage, 56&#8243; x 22&#8243; x 14&#8243;. Courtesy Anglim Gilbert Gallery.</p></div>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>Known for her sculptural installations and mixed media assemblage work, Mildred Howard has been the recipient of numerous awards, including the Adeline Kent Award from the San Francisco Art Institute, the Joan Mitchell Foundation and a fellowship from the California Arts Council. Her work has been exhibited internationally including recent shows in Berlin, Cairo, and Bath, England. Large scale installations have been mounted at the Richmond Art Center, Creative Time in New York, in SITE San Diego, the National Museum of Women in the Arts, and the New Museum in New York. Public commissions and installations were executed for the Museum of Glass in Tacoma, Washington, the City of Oakland, the San Francisco Arts Commission and International Airport, and the San Jose Museum of Art.</p>
<p><a href="http://anglimgilbertgallery.com/mildred-howard/#ms-2108" target="_blank">View artwork by Mildred Howard on the Anglim Gilbert Gallery website.</a></p>
<p style="text-align: center"><strong>* * * </strong></p>
<p><a href="https://www.facebook.com/UCBArtPractice">Like UC Berkeley Art Practice Events on Facebook</a></p>
<p><a href="https://www.instagram.com/worthryder/">Follow Worth Ryder Art Gallery on Instagram</a></p>
<p><a href="http://visitor.r20.constantcontact.com/d.jsp?llr=79l6t5cab&amp;p=oi&amp;m=1102686629724&amp;sit=f5ycjzneb&amp;f=58cb602a-5bbc-4389-925a-0dbde16f67d8">Join our email list</a> to receive invitations to exhibitions at the Worth Ryder Art Gallery, the Wiesenfeld Visiting Artist Lecture Series, and select Department of Art Practice events.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>

<article id="post-2928" class="group post-container entry-content">
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">The 47th Annual University of California, Berkeley Master of Fine Arts Graduate Exhibition, May 17 through June 11.</h1>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		
			
		<p>Each year, <a href="http://www.bampfa.berkeley.edu">BAMPFA</a> and the <a href="http://art.berkeley.edu/">University of California, Berkeley Department of Art Practice</a> work together to present an exhibition of works by Berkeley MFA graduates. This year’s graduates are <em>Takming Chuang, Lucas DeGiulio, Behnaz Khaleghi, Shari Paladino, Jovi Schnell</em>, and <em>Andrew Wilson</em>. Be among the first to encounter the recent work of these six exceptional artists.</p>
<p>There will also be an <a href="http://www.bampfa.berkeley.edu/event/mfa-artists%E2%80%99-talk">Artist&#8217;s Talk</a> and <a href="http://www.bampfa.berkeley.edu/event/mfa-opening-reception">Opening Reception</a> (the reception is for current BAMPFA members and guests of the artists) on <strong>May 17h</strong>.</p>
<p>Please the BAMPFA website for more details.</p>
<p><em><strong>The 47th Annual University of California, Berkeley Master of Fine Arts Graduate Exhibition</strong></em> is organized by Curatorial Assistant Matthew Coleman and Assistant Curator Stephanie Cannizzo. The annual MFA exhibition is made possible by the Barbara Berelson Wiltsek Endowment.</p>

	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->

</article><!-- #post-2928 -->

			
		
		</div><!-- #content-container -->


	</div><!-- #main -->

						<div id="sidebar" class="widget-area" role="complementary">
						<h2 class="widgettitle">Courses</h2>
<div class="menu-courses-sidebar-container"><ul id="menu-courses-sidebar" class="menu"><li id="menu-item-2939" class="menu-item menu-item-type-taxonomy menu-item-object-courses-category menu-item-2939"><a href="http://art.berkeley.edu/courses-category/2017-fall/">Fall 2017</a></li>
<li id="menu-item-1511" class="menu-item menu-item-type-taxonomy menu-item-object-courses-category menu-item-1511"><a href="http://art.berkeley.edu/courses-category/current-semester/">Spring 2017</a></li>
<li id="menu-item-2754" class="menu-item menu-item-type-taxonomy menu-item-object-courses-category menu-item-2754"><a href="http://art.berkeley.edu/courses-category/2016-fall/">Fall 2016</a></li>
<li id="menu-item-2758" class="menu-item menu-item-type-taxonomy menu-item-object-courses-category menu-item-2758"><a href="http://art.berkeley.edu/courses-category/2016-spring/">Spring 2016</a></li>
<li id="menu-item-1988" class="menu-item menu-item-type-taxonomy menu-item-object-courses-category menu-item-1988"><a href="http://art.berkeley.edu/courses-category/2015-fall/">Fall 2015</a></li>
<li id="menu-item-1521" class="menu-item menu-item-type-taxonomy menu-item-object-courses-category menu-item-1521"><a href="http://art.berkeley.edu/courses-category/2015-spring/">Spring 2015</a></li>
<li id="menu-item-1362" class="menu-item menu-item-type-taxonomy menu-item-object-courses-category menu-item-1362"><a href="http://art.berkeley.edu/courses-category/2014-fall/">Fall 2014</a></li>
<li id="menu-item-393" class="menu-item menu-item-type-taxonomy menu-item-object-courses-category menu-item-393"><a href="http://art.berkeley.edu/courses-category/2014-spring/">Spring 2014</a></li>
<li id="menu-item-392" class="menu-item menu-item-type-taxonomy menu-item-object-courses-category current-menu-item menu-item-392"><a href="http://art.berkeley.edu/courses-category/2013-fall/">Fall 2013</a></li>

				<header class="page-header">	
					<h1 class="page-title">2013 Fall</h1>
				</header>
		<section class="content-container">

			<div id="regular-content">
		
		<h2>Lower Division Courses</h2>
				
				
					<article id="post-101" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="index-title">
									Art 									8									: Introduction to Visual Thinking							</h1>
	
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p><em>*<strong>PLEASE NOTE</strong> that there are FOUR STUDIO SECTIONS.*</em><br />
This course will explore how we observe and interpret our visual world. We will examine material from a wide range of sources, focusing on the social, political, and cultural connections, as well as the conceptual base and formal properties that comprise a particular visual/sensory experience. Two ongoing concerns will be the exploration of how art and life intersect, and how our perceptions of what constitute the “high” and “low” in collective culture establish our beliefs about art. The course requires the completion of three projects that stress the visual, intellectual and intuitive aspects of art making.</p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-101 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-486" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="index-title">
									Art 									15									: Language of Sculpture: Ceramics							</h1>
	
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p><span style="font-family: Times">This class will investigate the potential of ceramics as sculpture.  Both traditional and experimental approaches to ceramics will be explored as a way to develop our technical skills and expand our conceptual approaches.  Field trips and illustrated talks will examine the ideas that have engaged ceramic sculptors in many traditions and the processes that they have used to expand them. Lectures and demonstrations introduce students to techniques and varied applications.</span></p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-486 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-402" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="index-title">
									Art 									23									: Digital Arts							</h1>
	
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p>This new course will enable students to think critically about, and engage in practical experiments in, the complex interactions between new media and perceptions and performances of embodiment, agency, citizenship, collective action, individual identity, time and spatiality. We will pay particular attention to the categories of personhood that make up the UC Berkeley American Cultures rubric (race and ethnicity), as well as to gender, nation, and disability. The argument threading through the course will be the ways in which new media both reinforce pre-existing social hierarchies, and yet offer possibilities for the transcendence of those very categories. The new media &#8212; and we will leave the precise definition of the new media as something to be argued about over the course of the semester &#8212; can be yet another means for dividing and disenfranchising, and can be the conduit of violence and transnational dominance.</p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-402 -->
	
															
					  
					

					
			<h2>Upper Division Courses</h2>
							
				
					<article id="post-484" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="index-title">
									Art 									123									: Approaches to Printmaking: Silkscreen							</h1>
	
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p>Course may be repeated for credit. Six hours of instructional studio and three hours of open studio per week. In the course of making screenprints, you will be encouraged to find an esthetic direction of your own. Your instructor will also help you develop skill in using both stone and metal plates.</p>
<p>Prerequisites: 12, 13, 14 or equivalents.</p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-484 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-410" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="index-title">
									Art 									138									: Installations							</h1>
	
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p>Course may be repeated for credit. Six hours of instructional studio and three hours of open studio per week. Installation and site-specific work, indoor and outdoor. Each student will select a site on campus and make a piece for that site. The choice of materials will depend on the exigencies of the site and of the student&#8217;s interest.</p>
<p>Prerequisites: 12, 13, 14 or equivalents.</p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-410 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-488" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="index-title">
									Art 									160									: Special Topics in Visual Studies: Material Worlds: Contemporary Sculpture and the Social Life of Objects.							</h1>
	
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<div class="text_exposed_show" style="color: #141823;font-family: Helvetica, Arial, 'lucida grande', tahoma, verdana, arial, sans-serif;font-size: 14px;line-height: 19.3199996948242px;text-align: left;background-color: #ffffff">
<p style="margin: 0px 0px 6px">This is an advanced interdisciplinary class with a focus on material culture, studio production, reading, and critical theory on capitalism’s effects on the production of art, as well as artists&#8217; responses to its demands.</p>
<p style="margin: 6px 0px">What does it mean to make “objects” in a world already glutted with object-products? How does the mechanical or mass production of object-products inform or create friction against the intimately handmade? This class will ask students to examine their own methods and materials of “object-making”, situate themselves against a contemporary context of global manufacturing and “product-making,” and attempt to reclaim the idea of sculpture as a social catalyst as opposed to standard consumer object.</p>
<p style="margin: 6px 0px">Topics covered will include the readymade and globalization, the unique object versus the mass-produced, artists who mimic corporate production and distribution chains, and the social and political effects of production, consumption, capitalism, and the various forms of resistance to it such as shopdropping, counterfeiting, public ownership, and the DIY craft movement. Art movements such as the Bauhaus and the Arts and Crafts Movement will be explored as examples of responses to the dilemma of mass production. We will also cover the intersection of art and design, looking at groups such as Atelier Van Lieshout, Unfold, Futurefarmers, and Andrea Zittel, among others.</p>
<p style="margin: 6px 0px">Students will utilize traditional sculptural mediums, ceramics, and craft techniques as well as simple 3-D computer modeling programs, free object databases, and digital processes, among others. Project assignments are structured to take advantage of individual students’ interests, while at the same time providing distinct parameters and goals. We will also be looking extensively at contemporary artists working today, and discussing how their art reflects upon and impacts the world around them. A museum field trip, collaborative project, visiting artist talks, and and workshops/demos will be included. Regular readings will be assigned, with discussion and response to follow.</p>
</div>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-488 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-487" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="index-title">
									Art 									160									: Special topics in Visual Studies: Projectors and Projections							</h1>
	
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p>Course may be repeated for credit. Six hours of instructional studio and three hours of open studio per week. Topics of concern to the instructor, usually related to current research, which may fall outside of the normal curriculum or be of more restricted content than regular studio courses. An opportunity to investigate topics and mediums on an ad hoc basis when there is a compelling reason to do so, providing there is no other course that deals with these concerns.</p>
<p>Prerequisite: Consent of instructor.</p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-487 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-413" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="index-title">
									Art 									174									: Advanced Digital Video							</h1>
	
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p>Nine hours of studio per week. This advanced studio course is designed for students who have mastered basic skills and concepts involved in digital video production and are interested in further investigating critical, theoretical, and creative research topics in digital video production. Also listed as Film and Media C187.</p>
<p>Prerequisites: Film 100, 185 with a grade of A- or better and consent of instructor.</p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-413 -->
	
										
					  
					 
					
							<h2>Graduate Courses</h2>
							</section><!-- #content-container -->

		</div>
		</div>
