		<h2>Lower Division Courses</h2>
				
				
					<article id="post-101" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="index-title">
									Art 									8									: Introduction to Visual Thinking							</h1>
	
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p><em>*<strong>PLEASE NOTE</strong> that there are FOUR STUDIO SECTIONS.*</em><br />
This course will explore how we observe and interpret our visual world. We will examine material from a wide range of sources, focusing on the social, political, and cultural connections, as well as the conceptual base and formal properties that comprise a particular visual/sensory experience. Two ongoing concerns will be the exploration of how art and life intersect, and how our perceptions of what constitute the “high” and “low” in collective culture establish our beliefs about art. The course requires the completion of three projects that stress the visual, intellectual and intuitive aspects of art making.</p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-101 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-102" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="index-title">
									Art 									12									: The Language of Drawing							</h1>
	
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p><em>*Please note that there are TWO sections for this course*</em></p>
<p>Six hours of instructional studio and three hours of open studio per week. A study of drawing as a tool for articulating what the eyes, hand, and mind discover and investigate when coordinated. Some sessions will be devoted to drawing the human figure.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-102 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-103" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="index-title">
									Art 									13									: The Language of Painting							</h1>
	
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p>Art 13 is an entry-level painting course designed to familiarize students with the materials, technical approaches, and processes of painting. Through a series of exercises, assignments, and visual presentations, we will investigate color interaction, contextual relationships, conceptual and compositional strategies, and the relationship between (subject, content, meaning) and process. Twentieth century painting history and theory will be referenced throughout our time together.</p>
<p>Consistent attendance is an important factor for this class to be a success. There will be a roster sheet for you to sign each day when you arrive. More than two absences will affect your final grade by one half. Two more absences will drop you another half.</p>
<div class="page" title="Page 1"></div>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-103 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-2014" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="index-title">
									Art 									14									: The Language of Sculpture &#8211; Mixed Media							</h1>
	
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p><span class="descshow overflow">This course is the study of the interaction between physical form and space. We will focus on building a strong conceptual foundation while developing the practical studio skills needed to translate your ideas into three dimensions. Shop practices will include hand, machine, and computer-aided fabrications. Field trips and illustrated talks will help acquaint students with the ideas sculptors have explored through history and in contemporary</span><span class="deschide"> sculptural practices.</span></p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-2014 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-486" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="index-title">
									Art 									15									: Language of Sculpture: Ceramics							</h1>
	
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p><span style="font-family: Times">This class will investigate the potential of ceramics as sculpture.  Both traditional and experimental approaches to ceramics will be explored as a way to develop our technical skills and expand our conceptual approaches.  Field trips and illustrated talks will examine the ideas that have engaged ceramic sculptors in many traditions and the processes that they have used to expand them. Lectures and demonstrations introduce students to techniques and varied applications.</span></p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-486 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-483" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="index-title">
									Art 									16									: Approaches to Printmaking							</h1>
	
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p>Six hours of instructional studio and three hours of open studio per week. This course examines and explores various print disciplines in a 15-week survey format. Students study and create traditional forms of fine art printmaking including woodcut, lithography, intaglio, and screenprinting as well as newer approaches which includes transfer and digital printmaking. Lectures and demonstrations introduce students to techniques and varied applications. This course is a prerequisite for upper division print courses.</p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-483 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-2941" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="index-title">
									Art 									21									: Beginning Digital Photo							</h1>
	
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p><span class="descshow">This is a basic foundation class for digital photography with hands-on instruction in the use of digital cameras and online image dissemination. Topics include image capture, composition, image syntax, image analysis, image manipulation, meta-text production, and image sequencing for visual narratives. We also study image dissemination through online networks-social networks, blogs, news, storage, search, and print services</span>.</p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-2941 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-402" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="index-title">
									Art 									23									: Digital Arts							</h1>
	
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p>This new course will enable students to think critically about, and engage in practical experiments in, the complex interactions between new media and perceptions and performances of embodiment, agency, citizenship, collective action, individual identity, time and spatiality. We will pay particular attention to the categories of personhood that make up the UC Berkeley American Cultures rubric (race and ethnicity), as well as to gender, nation, and disability. The argument threading through the course will be the ways in which new media both reinforce pre-existing social hierarchies, and yet offer possibilities for the transcendence of those very categories. The new media &#8212; and we will leave the precise definition of the new media as something to be argued about over the course of the semester &#8212; can be yet another means for dividing and disenfranchising, and can be the conduit of violence and transnational dominance.</p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-402 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-2942" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="index-title">
									Art A/Film									26									: Moving Image Media							</h1>
	
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p><span class="descshow">This course provides students with the technological and conceptual groundwork for advanced courses in video art and filmmaking including the use of digital cameras, sound recording, basic lighting techniques, digital editing, compression, and online dissemination. We will focus on what makes compelling moving images that elicit powerful intellectual and emotional responses. The course also explores</span><span class="deschide"> the range of techniques and languages of creative video making from traditional story genres to more contemporary experimental forms.<br />
The course consists of weekly lectures, screenings, discussions and a lab section. The lab is a production workshop in which students will produce a series of short exercises and a final project. </span></p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-2942 -->
	
															
					  
					

					
			<h2>Upper Division Courses</h2>
							
				
					<article id="post-403" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="index-title">
									Art 									102									: Approaches to Painting (section 002)							</h1>
	
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p>Often artists respond to limitations, whether self-imposed or life-imposed by lack of time, space or budget. But limitations are not always restrictions. Finding creative responses to limitations or boundaries can lead to unsuspected solutions. Many painters use specific limitations that propel their work into unexpected territories through their process, their materials or by finding ways of working that manifest as a result of an environmental or other constraint.</p>
<p>During this course, students will achieve the maximum impact within limited means. Through in-class and more in-depth projects, limitation exercises, and technical painting workshops, students will explore strategies that involve process, collaboration and project-based painting using limitation as a source of propulsion in their work. Emphasis will be placed on focusing one’s practice, as well as honing the skills needed to realize projects.</p>
<p>Students will study artists who utilize specialized processes, boundaries and strategies to guide and develop their work. The class will be supplemented by field trips and readings.</p>
<p><em>Course may be repeated for credit. Six hours of instructional studio and three hours open studio per week. Inquiry into concepts of order, process, and content as related to human experience. While faculty contact with students is highly individualized, the course involves group critiques and lectures as well as assigned field trips. <strong>Prerequisites</strong> 10, 12, and 14 or equivalents.</em></p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-403 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-2943" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="index-title">
									Art 									103									: Advanced Approaches to Painting							</h1>
	
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p><span class="descshow overflow">This is an advanced studio and lecture class for art majors, to advance their practice through practical research into the varied processes and methods of contemporary painting. Students will expand their skills and develop a dedicated practice through self generated projects and critical engagement. In-class critiques and open discussion will reinforce and challenge the students as a vital part of their technical, conceptual, and professional development. </span></p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-2943 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-406" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="index-title">
									Art 									118									: Contemporary Perspectives of Figure Drawing							</h1>
	
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p><span style="font-size: 10pt;font-family: Verdana">The primary focus of this course is to advance fundamental technical skills in the process of figure drawing, to gain understanding of human anatomy, and to acquire a personal visual language of drawing from life. Through close observation of the human form the student will develop ways of seeing that go beyond regular visual perception. The student will also expand the skills necessary for figure drawing executed in variety of dry and wet media, together with diverse methodologies of traditional and contemporary figure drawing.</span></p>
<p>Course may be repeated for credit. Six hours of instructional studio and three hours of open studio per week. Advanced drawing and composition, color and black-and-white, primarily on paper. Art 117 or 118 is required of <strong>all</strong> art majors.</p>
<p><strong>Prerequisites:</strong> 12, 13, and 14 or equivalents.</p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-406 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-2944" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="index-title">
									Art 									124									: Advanced Projects in Printmaking							</h1>
	
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p><span class="descshow">Non-traditional projects in printmaking. Lectures and demonstrations introduce students to techniques and varied applications. </span></p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-2944 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-485" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="index-title">
									Art 									138									: Approaches to Sculpture: Concept &amp; Construction							</h1>
	
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p><b> </b>In this class the student will consider sculptural issues of (and beyond) the object itself, notions of &#8220;site specific,&#8221; and of whether an object is distinct from its environment or is part of it. We will also question issues of space, placement, installation, context, and public interaction. Students will engage with a variety of sites, both on and off campus, with drawings and written proposals being an integral part of all projects. Lectures and demonstrations will introduce students to techniques and varied applications.</p>
<p>Prerequisites: 8, 12, and 14, or equivalents.</p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-485 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-405" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="index-title">
									Art 									160									: Ancient Pigments and Contemporary Drawing Practices							</h1>
	
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p><b>This is an advanced studio drawing course that compares and contrasts traditional Asian and European pigments, surfaces, and images with contemporary strategies, sources, and methods.  Students will use various traditional hand-made pigments, binders, papers, and drawing materials to explore cultural developments and representations of space, time, and objects. A major course outcome is to create hybrid, diverse content that combines contemporary images and narratives with traditional historic sources. The course provides lectures, demonstrations and studio research methodologies.</b></p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-405 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-1298" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="index-title">
									Art 									163									: Social Practice							</h1>
	
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p>Six hours of lecture and two hours of discussion per week. Social Practice broadly refers to work produced through various forms of direct engagement with a site, social system or collaborator. Interdisciplinary in nature, such work often takes the form of guerilla interventions, performance, institutional critique, community based public art and political activity, all sharing the premise that art created in the public sphere can help alter public perception and work toward social transformation.</p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-1298 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-495" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="index-title">
									Art 									171									: Beginning Digital Video							</h1>
	
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p>Nine hours of studio per week. Course may be repeated with consent of instructor. This course will cover a range of digital media and practices, with a view towards exploring current and future possibilities for photography. Inclusive of multiple approaches to scale, execution, and technique, the course enables students to examine and push the limits of photographic practices. This course will help students advance their digital shooting and Photoshop skills from a beginning to a more advanced level, and will cover the workflow of digital photography: camera usage, scanning, image editing, management, and printing.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-495 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-490" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="index-title">
									Art 									173									: Electro-Crafting							</h1>
	
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p>Six hours of lecture and three hours of studio per week. Course may be repeated for credit.<br />
This is a studio class designed to introduce artists to the medium of sound. Students will learn the basic skills necessary to work with audio, including microphones, digital recording, editing and processing, speaker and installation design, and circuit-bending. In addition, students will learn about the history of sound art and the ways in which visual art and experimental sound practice inform and expand upon each other.</p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-490 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-496" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="index-title">
									Art 									178									: Game Design Methods							</h1>
	
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p>Two hours of lecture and four hours of studio per week. This course offers an introduction to game design and game studies. Game studies has five core elements: the study of games as transmitters of culture, the study of play and interactivity, the study of games as symbolic systems; the study of games as artifacts; and methods for creating games. We will study these core elements through play, play tests, play analysis, and comparative studies. Our reading list includes classic game studies theory and texts which support game design methods. After weekly writing and design exercises, our coursework will culminate in the design and evaluation of an original code-based game with a tangible interface.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-496 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-2018" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="index-title">
									Art 									184									: Junior Seminar: Meaning and Making							</h1>
	
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p><span class="descshow overflow">This immersive studio/seminar class focuses on contemporary models of art making, exposing students to current issues in the art world, and fostering interdisciplinary models of thinking and making. Through field trips to museums, galleries, and alternative art spaces, as well as studio visits with local contemporary artists, students will be able to situate their own projects within the larger sphere of contemporary art. Language and writing skills around artist statements</span><span class="deschide">, critical readings, and the critique process will be emphasized to understand how research methods give meaning in a studio practice. Presentation of a final studio project asks students to examine their place within a contemporary art dialogue.</span></p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-2018 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-414" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="index-title">
									Art 									185									: Senior Projects							</h1>
	
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p>*Please note that there are TWO sections of this class, K185/001 AND K185/002</p>
<p>Three hours of lecture and three hours of studio critique per week. This course provides students with a foundation for understanding their work within a cross-disciplinary critical context. Through class and individual critique, readings, guest artists, and field trips, students will explore the practical and conceptual components of their own media and practice within a broader discussion of artistic production. In addition to this focused attention on the critique process, the class with address the ongoing needs of supporting one&#8217;s work within a community of artists, arts professionals, and arts organizations. Each student will work towards developing the most effective tools for communicating their work to these broader audiences using strategies that are appropriate/effective for their ideas, media, and audience.</p>
<p>Prerequisites: Senior level students only.</p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-414 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-2945" class="group index-post-container">

									Art BCNM									190									: Post-Secular: Religion, Ritual, and Performance							</h1>
	
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p>Please see UC Berkeley course catalog for details on this course.</p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-2945 -->
	
										
					  
					 
					
							<h2>Graduate Courses</h2>
							
				
					<article id="post-417" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="index-title">
									Art 									218									: Theory and Critique							</h1>
	
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p>Course may be repeated for credit. Hours to be arranged. <strong>ALL GRADUATE COURSES MUST BE TAKEN FOR A LETTER GRADE.</strong></p>
<p><strong>Prerequisites</strong><br />
Admission to the M.F.A. program. M.F.A. candidates, special study &#8211; M.F.A. Committee members as well as other faculty.</p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-417 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-491" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="index-title">
									Art 									294									: Seminar: Theory and Criticism							</h1>
	
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p>Course may be repeated for credit. Six hours of instructional studio and three hours open studio per week. Weekly meetings will provide a forum for the discussion of issues related to assigned readings in the fields of esthetics, theory and art criticism. <strong>ALL GRADUATE COURSES MUST BE TAKEN FOR A LETTER GRADE.</strong></p>
<p>Prerequisite: Graduate standing and consent of instructor.</p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-491 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-418" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="index-title">
									Art 									301									: The Teaching of Art: Practice							</h1>
	
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p style="margin: .1pt 0in .1pt 0in"><span style="font-size: 12.0pt;font-family: Cambria">The 301 Pedagogy Seminar is a preparation for GSI’s and is aligned directly with the teaching of Art 8: Introduction to Visual Thinking. Within our pre-semester pedagogy meetings, each graduate student develops a syllabus with emphasis on teaching philosophy, course content, approach to grading, attendance policy, and critique approach and style. Graduate students develop lesson plans and relational formal exercises for each of the course’s three main projects. Instructors receive the Art 8 Pedagogy manual, which contains twenty years of GSI’s syllabi and other helpful information. During our weekly semester pedagogy meetings, the graduate students and I discuss the pertinent issues of their current classroom dynamics. </span></p>
<p><strong>ALL GRADUATE COURSES MUST BE TAKEN FOR A LETTER GRADE.</strong></p>
<p><strong>Prerequisites</strong><br />
Consent of instructor.</p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-418 -->

		<section class="content-container">

			<div id="regular-content">
		
		<h2>Lower Division Courses</h2>
				
				
					<article id="post-101" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="course-title">
									Art 									8									: Introduction to Visual Thinking							</h1>
							
			<ul class="course-details-list">
				<li>Instructor: Various</li>
				<li>Room: K3645 or K395</li>
				<li>Time: 9-12pm OR 1-4pm</li>
			</ul>
													
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p><em>*<strong>PLEASE NOTE</strong> that there are FOUR STUDIO SECTIONS.*</em><br />
This course will explore how we observe and interpret our visual world. We will examine material from a wide range of sources, focusing on the social, political, and cultural connections, as well as the conceptual base and formal properties that comprise a particular visual/sensory experience. Two ongoing concerns will be the exploration of how art and life intersect, and how our perceptions of what constitute the “high” and “low” in collective culture establish our beliefs about art. The course requires the completion of three projects that stress the visual, intellectual and intuitive aspects of art making.</p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-101 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-102" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="course-title">
									Art 									12									: The Language of Drawing							</h1>
							
			<ul class="course-details-list">
				<li>Instructor: Nagasawa, Miki</li>
				<li>Room: K355 OR K365</li>
				<li>Time: 9am - noon, M/W/F </li>
			</ul>
													
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p><em>*Please note that there are TWO sections for this course*</em></p>
<p>Six hours of instructional studio and three hours of open studio per week. A study of drawing as a tool for articulating what the eyes, hand, and mind discover and investigate when coordinated. Some sessions will be devoted to drawing the human figure.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-102 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-103" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="course-title">
									Art 									13									: The Language of Painting							</h1>
							
			<ul class="course-details-list">
				<li>Instructor: McNamara</li>
				<li>Room: K385</li>
				<li>Time: 4-7pm M/W/F</li>
			</ul>
													
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p>Art 13 is an entry-level painting course designed to familiarize students with the materials, technical approaches, and processes of painting. Through a series of exercises, assignments, and visual presentations, we will investigate color interaction, contextual relationships, conceptual and compositional strategies, and the relationship between (subject, content, meaning) and process. Twentieth century painting history and theory will be referenced throughout our time together.</p>
<p>Consistent attendance is an important factor for this class to be a success. There will be a roster sheet for you to sign each day when you arrive. More than two absences will affect your final grade by one half. Two more absences will drop you another half.</p>
<div class="page" title="Page 1"></div>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-103 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-2014" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="course-title">
									Art 									14									: The Language of Sculpture &#8211; Mixed Media							</h1>
							
			<ul class="course-details-list">
				<li>Instructor: Hussong</li>
				<li>Room: K165</li>
				<li>Time: 9am-noon T/TH/S</li>
			</ul>
													
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p><span class="descshow overflow">This course is the study of the interaction between physical form and space. We will focus on building a strong conceptual foundation while developing the practical studio skills needed to translate your ideas into three dimensions. Shop practices will include hand, machine, and computer-aided fabrications. Field trips and illustrated talks will help acquaint students with the ideas sculptors have explored through history and in contemporary</span><span class="deschide"> sculptural practices.</span></p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					<article id="post-486" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="course-title">
									Art 									15									: Language of Sculpture: Ceramics							</h1>
							
			<ul class="course-details-list">
				<li>Instructor: TBD</li>
				<li>Room: W178</li>
				<li>Time: 9am-Noon M/W/F</li>
			</ul>
													
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p><span style="font-family: Times">This class will investigate the potential of ceramics as sculpture.  Both traditional and experimental approaches to ceramics will be explored as a way to develop our technical skills and expand our conceptual approaches.  Field trips and illustrated talks will examine the ideas that have engaged ceramic sculptors in many traditions and the processes that they have used to expand them. Lectures and demonstrations introduce students to techniques and varied applications.</span></p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-486 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-483" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="course-title">
									Art 									16									: Approaches to Printmaking							</h1>
							
			<ul class="course-details-list">
				<li>Instructor: Hussong</li>
				<li>Room: K265</li>
				<li>Time: 9am - Noon M/W/F</li>
			</ul>
													
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p>Six hours of instructional studio and three hours of open studio per week. This course examines and explores various print disciplines in a 15-week survey format. Students study and create traditional forms of fine art printmaking including woodcut, lithography, intaglio, and screenprinting as well as newer approaches which includes transfer and digital printmaking. Lectures and demonstrations introduce students to techniques and varied applications. This course is a prerequisite for upper division print courses.</p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-483 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-2941" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="course-title">
									Art 									21									: Beginning Digital Photo							</h1>
							
			<ul class="course-details-list">
				<li>Instructor: TBD</li>
				<li>Room: 275</li>
				<li>Time: 1pm-4pm, T/TH/S</li>
			</ul>
													
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p><span class="descshow">This is a basic foundation class for digital photography with hands-on instruction in the use of digital cameras and online image dissemination. Topics include image capture, composition, image syntax, image analysis, image manipulation, meta-text production, and image sequencing for visual narratives. We also study image dissemination through online networks-social networks, blogs, news, storage, search, and print services</span>.</p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-2941 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-402" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="course-title">
									Art 									23									: Digital Arts							</h1>
							
			<ul class="course-details-list">
				<li>Instructor: Niemeyer</li>
				<li>Room: given online</li>
				<li>Time: online course</li>
			</ul>
													
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p>This new course will enable students to think critically about, and engage in practical experiments in, the complex interactions between new media and perceptions and performances of embodiment, agency, citizenship, collective action, individual identity, time and spatiality. We will pay particular attention to the categories of personhood that make up the UC Berkeley American Cultures rubric (race and ethnicity), as well as to gender, nation, and disability. The argument threading through the course will be the ways in which new media both reinforce pre-existing social hierarchies, and yet offer possibilities for the transcendence of those very categories. The new media &#8212; and we will leave the precise definition of the new media as something to be argued about over the course of the semester &#8212; can be yet another means for dividing and disenfranchising, and can be the conduit of violence and transnational dominance.</p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-402 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-2942" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="course-title">
									Art A/Film									26									: Moving Image Media							</h1>
							
			<ul class="course-details-list">
				<li>Instructor: Skoller</li>
				<li>Room: Dwinelle 142</li>
				<li>Time: 1pm-4PM</li>
			</ul>
													
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p><span class="descshow">This course provides students with the technological and conceptual groundwork for advanced courses in video art and filmmaking including the use of digital cameras, sound recording, basic lighting techniques, digital editing, compression, and online dissemination. We will focus on what makes compelling moving images that elicit powerful intellectual and emotional responses. The course also explores</span><span class="deschide"> the range of techniques and languages of creative video making from traditional story genres to more contemporary experimental forms.<br />
The course consists of weekly lectures, screenings, discussions and a lab section. The lab is a production workshop in which students will produce a series of short exercises and a final project. </span></p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

			<h2>Upper Division Courses</h2>
							
				
					<article id="post-3062" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="course-title">
									Art 									100									: ART 100: COLLABORATIVE INNOVATION							</h1>
	<ul class="course-details-list">
				<li>Instructor: Chuang</li>
				<li>Room: 310 Jacobs Hall </li>
				<li>Time: 2 PM - 5 PM T/TH</li>
			</ul>
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p>In this hands-on, project-based class in collaborative innovation, students will experience group creativity and team-based design by using techniques from across the disciplines of business, theatre, design, and art practice. They will leverage problem framing and solving techniques derived from critical thinking, systems thinking, and creative problem solving (popularly known today as “design thinking”). The course is grounded in a brief weekly lecture that sets out the theoretical, historical, and cultural contexts for particular innovation practices, but the majority of the class involves hands-on studio-based learning guided by an interdisciplinary team of teachers leading small group collaborative projects. Students will experience observation, problem-framing, divergent and convergent thinking, iterative solution testing, improvisation, storytelling, devised theatre, and public speaking and presentation activities. By engaging in these activities, the course provides students with the opportunity to develop new mindsets, skill-sets and tool sets for use in collaborative innovation efforts.</p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-3062 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-2741" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="course-title">
									Art 									100									: Collaborative Innovation: Big Ideas							</h1>
	<ul class="course-details-list">
				<li>Instructor: TBD</li>
				<li>Room: Jacobs Hall</li>
				<li>Time: T/TH 2-5pm</li>
			</ul>
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p><span class="descshow">In this hands-on, project-based class, students will experience group creativity and team-based design by using techniques from across the disciplines of business, theatre, design, and art practice. They will leverage problem framing and solving techniques derived from critical thinking, systems thinking, and creative problem solving (popularly known today as design thinking). The course is grounded in a brief weekly lecture that sets out</span><span class="deschide"> the theoretical, historical, and cultural contexts for particular innovation practices, but the majority of the class involves hands-on studio-based learning guided by an interdisciplinary team of teachers leading small group collaborative projects. </span></p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					<article id="post-403" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="course-title">
									Art 									102									: Approaches to Painting (section 002)							</h1>
	<ul class="course-details-list">
				<li>Instructor: Morre</li>
				<li>Room: K385</li>
				<li>Time: 9am-noon M/W/F </li>
			</ul>
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p>Often artists respond to limitations, whether self-imposed or life-imposed by lack of time, space or budget. But limitations are not always restrictions. Finding creative responses to limitations or boundaries can lead to unsuspected solutions. Many painters use specific limitations that propel their work into unexpected territories through their process, their materials or by finding ways of working that manifest as a result of an environmental or other constraint.</p>
<p>During this course, students will achieve the maximum impact within limited means. Through in-class and more in-depth projects, limitation exercises, and technical painting workshops, students will explore strategies that involve process, collaboration and project-based painting using limitation as a source of propulsion in their work. Emphasis will be placed on focusing one’s practice, as well as honing the skills needed to realize projects.</p>
<p>Students will study artists who utilize specialized processes, boundaries and strategies to guide and develop their work. The class will be supplemented by field trips and readings.</p>
<p><em>Course may be repeated for credit. Six hours of instructional studio and three hours open studio per week. Inquiry into concepts of order, process, and content as related to human experience. While faculty contact with students is highly individualized, the course involves group critiques and lectures as well as assigned field trips. <strong>Prerequisites</strong> 10, 12, and 14 or equivalents.</em></p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-403 -->

Students will expand their skills and develop a dedicated practice through self generated projects and critical engagement. In-class critiques and open discussion will reinforce and challenge the students as a vital part of their technical, conceptual, and professional development. </span></p>

					<article id="post-406" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="course-title">
									Art 									118									: Contemporary Perspectives of Figure Drawing							</h1>
	<ul class="course-details-list">
				<li>Instructor: Morre</li>
				<li>Room: K355</li>
				<li>Time: 1-4pm, M/W/F</li>
			</ul>
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p><span style="font-size: 10pt;font-family: Verdana">The primary focus of this course is to advance fundamental technical skills in the process of figure drawing, to gain understanding of human anatomy, and to acquire a personal visual language of drawing from life. Through close observation of the human form the student will develop ways of seeing that go beyond regular visual perception. The student will also expand the skills necessary for figure drawing executed in variety of dry and wet media, together with diverse methodologies of traditional and contemporary figure drawing.</span></p>
<p>Course may be repeated for credit. Six hours of instructional studio and three hours of open studio per week. Advanced drawing and composition, color and black-and-white, primarily on paper. Art 117 or 118 is required of <strong>all</strong> art majors.</p>
<p><strong>Prerequisites:</strong> 12, 13, and 14 or equivalents.</p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-406 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-407" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="course-title">
									Art 									119									: Global Perspectives in Contemporary Art							</h1>
	<ul class="course-details-list">
				<li>Instructor: deSouza</li>
				<li>Room: K120</li>
				<li>Time: 4-7pm, Th</li>
			</ul>
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p><span class="descshow">This course is designed to explore a range of contemporary art movements around the globe, through a closer look at their central ideas, artists, and artworks, as well as the preconditions and broader social context in which the work is being produced. Topics covered will range from the emergence of localized avant-garde movements in Europe, Asia, Africa, and Latin America to the implicit globalism</span><span class="deschide"> of the international biennial circuit. </span></p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					<article id="post-492" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="course-title">
									Art 									120									: Advanced Projects: Print Intaglio							</h1>
	<ul class="course-details-list">
				<li>Instructor: Hussong</li>
				<li>Room: K265</li>
				<li>Time: 1-4pm, M/W/F</li>
			</ul>
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p>Six hours of lecture and three hours of studio per week. The process of Intaglio will be explored in a variety of image producing techniques. Hand drawn, photographic, and digitally manipulated images are combined to produce multiple works of limited edition fine art prints. Image content and development is examined through drawings, studies, slide lectures, group critiques, and direct assistance. Each student is required to attend all class periods and participate in group discussions and critique. It is the responsibility of the student to maintain a portfolio of all works executed during the semester and to turn in all assignments on time. The grade is determined by attendance, completion of projects and participation in critiques. Personal improvement will also be taken into account.</p>
<p>Prerequisites: Open to upper division art majors or by consent of instructor.<br />
Credit option: Course may be repeated for credit.</p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					<article id="post-3063" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="course-title">
									Art L&S									128									: CROWDS &amp; CLOUDS							</h1>
	<ul class="course-details-list">
				<li>Instructor: Walsh</li>
				<li>Room: GSPP 150</li>
				<li>Time: 11:00 - 12: 30PM T/TH</li>
			</ul>
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<div id="gmail-m_6268622406234940629gmail-description">
<p>What is a crowd? Is it a swarm of bodies on the street, a dancing flash mob, or a set of data points culled from social media? This class investigates how digital cloud technologies cluster us together into formations that could be understood as both pleasurable and dangerous. We will look at how visual artists and social theorists have addressed issues such as self-tracking, fears around immigration, and crowdsourcing. We will also investigate how surveillance, big data, and social media are used by current governments and reframed by insurgent political movements. From the ubiquity of cloud computing to the crowds that have redefined contemporary mass politics, innovations in media platforms, data collection, and digital labor are redefining the ways that collectives are imagined, produced and defined. Crowds and Clouds examines the shared trajectories of technology and sociality, approaching the cloud and crowd through historically grounded, interdisciplinary scholarship in the visual arts, art history, ethnography, literature, and geography.</p>
</div>
<p>The course has no prerequisites and is open to all undergraduates, but anyone enrolling should be prepared to think hard, read deeply, question preconceived notions, and work.</p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-3063 -->

					<article id="post-485" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="course-title">
									Art 									138									: Approaches to Sculpture: Concept &amp; Construction							</h1>
	<ul class="course-details-list">
				<li>Instructor: Reiman</li>
				<li>Room: K165</li>
				<li>Time: 1-4pm, T/Th/S</li>
			</ul>
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p><b> </b>In this class the student will consider sculptural issues of (and beyond) the object itself, notions of &#8220;site specific,&#8221; and of whether an object is distinct from its environment or is part of it. We will also question issues of space, placement, installation, context, and public interaction. Students will engage with a variety of sites, both on and off campus, with drawings and written proposals being an integral part of all projects. Lectures and demonstrations will introduce students to techniques and varied applications.</p>
<p>Prerequisites: 8, 12, and 14, or equivalents.</p>

					<article id="post-3061" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="course-title">
									Art 									160									: ART 160 &#8211; ART &amp; POLITICS							</h1>
	<ul class="course-details-list">
				<li>Instructor: Garcia</li>
				<li>Room: K120 </li>
				<li>Time: 9AM - 12PM T/TH/S  </li>
			</ul>
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p>This multidisciplinary studio course will investigate the intertwined<br />
histories of art and politics, the powerful relationship that contemporary<br />
art and politics continue to share, and how artists strive to create<br />
social change by responding directly to current affairs and global<br />
political issues.<br />
Class projects and lectures will explore the growing number of artists,<br />
activists, and movements that are working together to change the<br />
political landscape, and how art has historically helped produce<br />
dissent and bring attention to issues and concerns that shape the<br />
global community.<i><b><u><br />
</u></b></i></p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-3061 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-405" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="course-title">
									Art 									160									: Ancient Pigments and Contemporary Drawing Practices							</h1>
	<ul class="course-details-list">
				<li>Instructor: Nagasawa</li>
				<li>Room: K365</li>
				<li>Time: 1-4pm, T/Th/S</li>
			</ul>
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p><b>This is an advanced studio drawing course that compares and contrasts traditional Asian and European pigments, surfaces, and images with contemporary strategies, sources, and methods.  Students will use various traditional hand-made pigments, binders, papers, and drawing materials to explore cultural developments and representations of space, time, and objects. A major course outcome is to create hybrid, diverse content that combines contemporary images and narratives with traditional historic sources. The course provides lectures, demonstrations and studio research methodologies.</b></p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-405 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-1298" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="course-title">
									Art 									163									: Social Practice							</h1>
	<ul class="course-details-list">
				<li>Instructor: Syjuco</li>
				<li>Room: W178</li>
				<li>Time: 1pm-4pm, M/W/F</li>
			</ul>
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p>Six hours of lecture and two hours of discussion per week. Social Practice broadly refers to work produced through various forms of direct engagement with a site, social system or collaborator. Interdisciplinary in nature, such work often takes the form of guerilla interventions, performance, institutional critique, community based public art and political activity, all sharing the premise that art created in the public sphere can help alter public perception and work toward social transformation.</p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-1298 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-495" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="course-title">
									Art 									171									: Beginning Digital Video							</h1>
	<ul class="course-details-list">
				<li>Instructor: Seraj</li>
				<li>Room: K285</li>
				<li>Time: 1pm-4pm, M/W/F</li>
			</ul>
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p>Nine hours of studio per week. Course may be repeated with consent of instructor. This course will cover a range of digital media and practices, with a view towards exploring current and future possibilities for photography. Inclusive of multiple approaches to scale, execution, and technique, the course enables students to examine and push the limits of photographic practices. This course will help students advance their digital shooting and Photoshop skills from a beginning to a more advanced level, and will cover the workflow of digital photography: camera usage, scanning, image editing, management, and printing.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-495 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-490" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="course-title">
									Art 									173									: Electro-Crafting							</h1>
	<ul class="course-details-list">
				<li>Instructor: Kubick</li>
				<li>Room: K285</li>
				<li>Time: 9am-noon, T/Th/S</li>
			</ul>
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p>Six hours of lecture and three hours of studio per week. Course may be repeated for credit.<br />
This is a studio class designed to introduce artists to the medium of sound. Students will learn the basic skills necessary to work with audio, including microphones, digital recording, editing and processing, speaker and installation design, and circuit-bending. In addition, students will learn about the history of sound art and the ways in which visual art and experimental sound practice inform and expand upon each other.</p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-490 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-496" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="course-title">
									Art 									178									: Game Design Methods							</h1>
	<ul class="course-details-list">
				<li>Instructor: Niemeyer</li>
				<li>Room: K285</li>
				<li>Time: 9am-noon, M/W/F</li>
			</ul>
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p>Two hours of lecture and four hours of studio per week. This course offers an introduction to game design and game studies. Game studies has five core elements: the study of games as transmitters of culture, the study of play and interactivity, the study of games as symbolic systems; the study of games as artifacts; and methods for creating games. We will study these core elements through play, play tests, play analysis, and comparative studies. Our reading list includes classic game studies theory and texts which support game design methods. After weekly writing and design exercises, our coursework will culminate in the design and evaluation of an original code-based game with a tangible interface.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-496 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-1349" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="course-title">
									Art 									180									: Advanced Digital Photography							</h1>
	<ul class="course-details-list">
				<li>Instructor: Ott</li>
				<li>Room: K275</li>
				<li>Time: T/TH 9am-noon</li>
			</ul>
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p><strong>Course Format:</strong> Two hours of lecture and seven hours of studio per week.<br />
<strong>Description:</strong> This class provides a basic foundation for digital photography with hands-on instruction in the use of digital cameras and online image dissemination. Topics include image capture, composition, image syntax, image analysis, image manipulation, metatext production, and image sequencing for visual narratives. We also study image dissemination through online networks including social networks, blogs, news, storage, search, and print services. Rather than limiting the discussion of photography to the production of the photographic image itself, we explore in written assignments how the reception of images can change based on context, usage, and network dynamics. While we rely on required DSLR digital cameras to produce images for weekly photographic assignments, we also experiment with alternate digital image generation techniques from telescopes to microscopes. All coursework will be posted and discussed online as well as in weekly lectures, workshops, and critiques. Course readings cover the history of photography, the theory of photographic reproduction and the theory of networked and memetic dissemination</p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-1349 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-2018" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="course-title">
									Art 									184									: Junior Seminar: Meaning and Making							</h1>
	<ul class="course-details-list">
				<li>Instructor: Reiman</li>
				<li>Room: K165</li>
				<li>Time: 4pm-7pm T/TH/S</li>
			</ul>
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p><span class="descshow overflow">This immersive studio/seminar class focuses on contemporary models of art making, exposing students to current issues in the art world, and fostering interdisciplinary models of thinking and making. Through field trips to museums, galleries, and alternative art spaces, as well as studio visits with local contemporary artists, students will be able to situate their own projects within the larger sphere of contemporary art. Language and writing skills around artist statements</span><span class="deschide">, critical readings, and the critique process will be emphasized to understand how research methods give meaning in a studio practice. Presentation of a final studio project asks students to examine their place within a contemporary art dialogue.</span></p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-2018 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-414" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="course-title">
									Art 									185									: Senior Projects							</h1>
	<ul class="course-details-list">
				<li>Instructor: K185/001, Syjuco. K185/002, Walsh</li>
				<li>Room: K120</li>
				<li>Time: K185/001: 4pm-7pm M/W/F, K185/002 1pm-4pm T/TH/S</li>
			</ul>
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p>*Please note that there are TWO sections of this class, K185/001 AND K185/002</p>
<p>Three hours of lecture and three hours of studio critique per week. This course provides students with a foundation for understanding their work within a cross-disciplinary critical context. Through class and individual critique, readings, guest artists, and field trips, students will explore the practical and conceptual components of their own media and practice within a broader discussion of artistic production. In addition to this focused attention on the critique process, the class with address the ongoing needs of supporting one&#8217;s work within a community of artists, arts professionals, and arts organizations. Each student will work towards developing the most effective tools for communicating their work to these broader audiences using strategies that are appropriate/effective for their ideas, media, and audience.</p>
<p>Prerequisites: Senior level students only.</p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-414 -->

							<h2>Graduate Courses</h2>
							
				
					<article id="post-417" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="course-title">
									Art 									218									: Theory and Critique							</h1>
	<ul class="course-details-list">
				<li>Instructor: Walsh</li>
				<li>Room: RFS</li>
				<li>Time: 5-8pm T</li>
			</ul>
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p>Course may be repeated for credit. Hours to be arranged. <strong>ALL GRADUATE COURSES MUST BE TAKEN FOR A LETTER GRADE.</strong></p>
<p><strong>Prerequisites</strong><br />
Admission to the M.F.A. program. M.F.A. candidates, special study &#8211; M.F.A. Committee members as well as other faculty.</p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-417 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-491" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="course-title">
									Art 									294									: Seminar: Theory and Criticism							</h1>
	<ul class="course-details-list">
				<li>Instructor: Kazmi</li>
				<li>Room: K346</li>
				<li>Time: 5pm-8pm, TH</li>
			</ul>
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p>Course may be repeated for credit. Six hours of instructional studio and three hours open studio per week. Weekly meetings will provide a forum for the discussion of issues related to assigned readings in the fields of esthetics, theory and art criticism. <strong>ALL GRADUATE COURSES MUST BE TAKEN FOR A LETTER GRADE.</strong></p>
<p>Prerequisite: Graduate standing and consent of instructor.</p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-491 -->
	
					
					<article id="post-418" class="group index-post-container">
						<header class="index-header">
							<h1 class="course-title">
									Art 									301									: The Teaching of Art: Practice							</h1>
	<ul class="course-details-list">
				<li>Instructor: McNamara</li>
				<li>Room: K338</li>
				<li>Time: 12-1pm Wed</li>
			</ul>
						</header><!-- .entry-header -->

						<div class="index-content">
							<p style="margin: .1pt 0in .1pt 0in"><span style="font-size: 12.0pt;font-family: Cambria">The 301 Pedagogy Seminar is a preparation for GSI’s and is aligned directly with the teaching of Art 8: Introduction to Visual Thinking. Within our pre-semester pedagogy meetings, each graduate student develops a syllabus with emphasis on teaching philosophy, course content, approach to grading, attendance policy, and critique approach and style. Graduate students develop lesson plans and relational formal exercises for each of the course’s three main projects. Instructors receive the Art 8 Pedagogy manual, which contains twenty years of GSI’s syllabi and other helpful information. During our weekly semester pedagogy meetings, the graduate students and I discuss the pertinent issues of their current classroom dynamics. </span></p>
<p><strong>ALL GRADUATE COURSES MUST BE TAKEN FOR A LETTER GRADE.</strong></p>
<p><strong>Prerequisites</strong><br />
Consent of instructor.</p>
						</div><!-- .entry-content -->

					</article><!-- #post-418 -->

				<img src=" http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2015/11/GradStudioBanner.jpg ">

	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Graduate Studios</h1>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		<h2><strong>UC and Richmond Global Campus</strong></h2>
<p>The UC and Richmond Global Campus (formerly the Richmond Field Station) provides over 10,000 square feet of studio space in a beautiful San Francisco Bay-side location. Available for 24-hour use, the UC and Richmond Global Campus  (RGC) graduate studios share a kitchen, a meeting room, open construction space, wireless internet access, a digital video editing station, digital printers, and flexible exhibition space.</p>
<p>Although there is a bus from campus to the UC and Richmond Global Campus, it does not run after 6pm or on weekends and the public transportation system does not provide service to the site at this time. <em><strong>Students need to have their own transportation to have adequate access to the studios.</strong></em></p>
<p><strong>Please note: we <span style="text-decoration: underline">do not</span> give tours of the MFA studios.</strong></p>
<h2>UC Berkeley Main Campus</h2>
<p>Two graduate studios are maintained in Wurster Hall on the UC Berkeley main campus. Both studios are wheelchair-accessible.</p>

<article id="post-648" class="group post-container entry-content">
	

			<div class="page-banner">
				<img src=" http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2013/10/wrag1.jpg ">
			</div>

		
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Internship Program</h1>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		<p><strong>The Worth Ryder Art Gallery Internship program is an opportunity to learn how art exhibitions are put together, to gain experience in the day-to-day functioning of an art gallery from the inside, and to work with professional curators and artists.  </strong></p>
<p>The internship is a two-credit pass/fail course. Attendance and punctuality are essential to passing. Internships are open to ALL UC Berkeley Students, NOT limited to Art Practice majors or upper division students. The application process is competitive.</p>
<p><a href="http://art.berkeley.edu/events/event/internship_2017/"><strong>The Application for the Fall 2017 Program is now live.</strong></a></p>
<p>Students are asked to take the internship program for two credits and play two roles: Attendant/Host and Preparator/Publicity. Students should expect to commit five hours per week to the program.</p>
<p><strong>Attendant/Host</strong></p>
<p>Each student who signs up for the program is required to be present to attend the gallery for one 2.5 hour shift per week during the semester OR help host receptions and artist talks. <strong>Attendant</strong> shifts are scheduled during gallery hours, 12 – 5pm, Monday – Thursday, while <strong>Host</strong> shifts are odd hours.</p>
<p><strong>Preparator/Publicity</strong></p>
<p>Additionally, each intern takes on two specialized positions. These positions are scheduled flexibly, but do require students to be present for hours during installation periods and at meetings with the Director.</p>
<p>As a <strong>Preparator </strong>you will be installing exhibitions for approximately 4-hour shifts before each of five exhibitions. You will be hanging work, painting, organizing, making gallery materials, assisting professional and student artists, and will gain valuable hands-on experience. You will be trained in the use of basic tools used to install exhibitions. No experience required.</p>
<p>As a <strong>Publicity Interns</strong> you will help the Director prepare PR materials, publicize events, document exhibitions, enter exhibition descriptions into online arts calendars, contact local arts media, prepare content for our website and social media, and make things BIG.</p>
<p style="text-align: center"><strong>***</strong></p>
<h2 style="text-align: left">INTERESTED IN APPLYING?</h2>
<p><a href="http://art.berkeley.edu/events/event/internship_2017/"><strong>The Application for the Fall 2017 Program is now live.</strong></a></p>
<p style="text-align: left">QUESTIONS? Please write to Program Director Farley Gwazda: <a  href="javascript:smae_decode('Z3dhemRhQGJlcmtlbGV5LmVkdQ==');" >&#103;&#119;&#097;&#122;&#100;&#097;&#064;&#098;&#101;&#114;&#107;&#101;&#108;&#101;&#121;&#046;&#101;&#100;&#117;</a></p>
<p><a href="http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2013/11/level.jpg" rel="lightbox[648]" title="Internship Program"><img class="aligncenter wp-image-1615" src="http://art.berkeley.edu/files/2013/11/level-300x231.jpg" alt="level" width="558" height="429" /></a></p>
	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->
</article><!-- #post-648 -->

<article id="post-32" class="group post-container entry-content">
	
				
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Programs</h1>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		<p>The Department of Art Practice at UC Berkeley offers the intense focus of a professional art school within the context of a world-renowned public research university. Students are challenged to pursue creative research across disciplines. We offer both the Bachelor of Arts (B.A.) degree and the Master of Fine Arts (MFA) degree.</p>
	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->
</article><!-- #post-32 -->

<article id="post-65" class="group post-container entry-content">
	
				
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Art Practice History</h1>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		<p>When the University of California was founded in 1869, courses in painting, sculpture, and drawing were part of the curricula of the Architecture Department, Drawing and Decorative Arts Department, the Department of Domestic Art, and the Department of Drawing and Mapping. It was not until the early 20th-century that the Department of Art Practice came into being.</p>
<p>In 1915, Assistant Professor of Decorative Design in the Department of Domestic Art, Eugene Neuhaus, lectured on art at the Panama-Pacific International Exposition. His lectures at the Exposition, and during the years following, generated so much statewide interest in contemporary fine art that by 1923 the Department of Art Practice was established on the Berkeley campus under the Chairmanship of Professor Eugene Neuhaus. In the 1930&#8217;s and 1940&#8217;s, Oliver Washburn, Stephen Pepper and Worth Ryder also served as department Chairs.</p>
<p>During the Department of Art Practice&#8217;s 1930 and 1931 summer sessions, Hans Hoffmann electrified students and the Bay Area community with his concepts on modern art. Subsequently, he donated 45 of his paintings to UC Berkeley. These paintings became the basis for the Berkeley Art Museum&#8217;s permanent Hans Hoffmann collection. Internationally recognized artist, Chiura Obata, was a member of the Art Practice faculty from 1932-1954. In addition to Hoffmann and Obata, the department&#8217;s faculty has included nationally and internationally acclaimed artists such as Joan Brown, Jay deFeo, David Hockney, Sylvia Lark, and Peter Voulkos. More recent distinguished artists on our faculty include Visiting Lecturers Richard Diebenkorn, Enrique Chagoya, Vincent Fecteau, Monica Majoli, Helen Mirra, Kota Ezawa, and Marianne Weems.</p>
<p>The department&#8217;s alumni have used their UC Berkeley training in the visual fine arts as a preparation for careers in advertising, fashion design, gallery entrepreneurship, film editing, film production, museum and gallery curating, graphics, publishing, and theater/film set design and construction. Our alumni have gone on to teach at the Academy of Art College, Alfred University, Bennington College, California College of Arts and Crafts, California State University, Carnegie-Mellon University, Chicago Art Institute, Johns Hopkins University, Memphis State University, Notre Dame University, Parsons School of Design, Princeton University, Rutgers University, San Francisco Art Institute, San Francisco School of Art, San Francisco State University, Stanford University, University of California, University of Minnesota, and Yale University.</p>
	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->
</article><!-- #post-65 -->

										</div>
			</div><!-- #content -->



	</div><!-- #main -->

<article id="post-200" class="group post-container entry-content">
	
				
	<header class="entry-header">
		<h1 class="entry-title">Graduate Program</h1>
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	<div class="entry-content-wrapper">
		<p><em>For a comprehensive Q&amp;A on our program, please see our <a href="http://art.berkeley.edu/uc-berkeley-mfa-program-information/">Q&amp;A page.</a><br />
</em></p>
<p>To begin your application process, please see the <strong><a href="http://grad.berkeley.edu/admissions/apply/">Graduate Division</a></strong> website.</p>
<p><em><strong>Our MFA Program</strong></em></p>
<p>The two-year Master of Fine Arts (MFA) Program supports 12 graduate students per year, and strives to graduate working artists who will continue to demonstrate significant artistic, critical and cultural impacts across a wide array of disciplines. Incoming MFA students are expected to be already deeply engaged in their creative practice and possess technical proficiency in their chosen media. Graduate coursework and independent study are designed to help students develop a critical understanding of their creative work in the multiple contexts of specific localities and global contemporary art. Our graduate students are encouraged to take university-wide and cross-disciplinary courses linked to their research interests, studying and collaborating with faculty and graduate students in areas as diverse as Geography, Environmental Sciences, Classics, Art History, Disability Studies, Philosophy, Cognitive Science, Interactive Design, Rhetoric, Film Studies, and Comparative Literature.</p>
<p>The Program seeks to help students develop a keen sense of their audience and to consider how they will reach, or generate, that audience for their work. Art Practice faculty will help to facilitate the exposure of graduate student work to a broader public, whether in museums, galleries, public places, or through other forms of engagement and dissemination.</p>
<p>Peer-to-peer discussion and critique form the heart of Berkeley’s MFA Program. Students respond to their classmates’ work, and learn to think, speak and write critically about art’s functions and possibilities.  A <a href="http://art.berkeley.edu/events/category/wiesenfeld-visiting-artist-lecture-series/page/2/">Visiting Artist Lecture Series</a>, along with studio visits, offer graduate students the chance to connect with internationally-known artists. Students also will have the opportunity to teach in the second year, mentored and closely supported by a faculty member.  Exhibitions in the first and second year of study require students to maintain a rigorous pace of creative research and establish a professional art practice. The final thesis exhibition, completed after the second year of study, is held at the Berkeley Art Museum.</p>
<p>Why an MFA from Berkeley?</p>
<p><em><strong>INNOVATION</strong></em></p>
<p>Art Practice is committed to artistic innovation and intellectual rigor. We emphasize critical thinking and strongly encourage work that rethinks context, material form, and conceptual approach. The program aims to broaden the productive, conceptual, and critical faculties of students in their pursuit of careers as professional artists.</p>
<p><em><strong>FACULTY</strong></em></p>
<p>The department’s faculty consists of established and emerging artists. All students work closely with individual faculty members through a system of independent study and programmatic coursework. Every student is guided through the program by a faculty mentor, who serves as that student’s artistic and academic advisor.</p>
<p><em><strong>ACADEMIC EXCELLENCE</strong></em></p>
<p>The program emphasizes making within a highly rigorous conceptual and critical approach, and is designed to prepare students for a professional exhibiting career and for teaching at the college level. The curriculum emphasizes the richness of UC Berkeley’s broader research community, and close working relationships with faculty. Opportunities exist for assisted and faculty-accompanied travel abroad.</p>
<p><strong><em>INTERDISCIPLINARITY</em></strong></p>
<p>The MFA Program promotes interdisciplinarity, with a facility to pursue and realize ideas through the most appropriate media. Students test ideas and work in a wide range of media and disciplines, which include but are not limited to broadly defined areas of painting, drawing, sculpture, photography, video, printmaking, digital and new media, performance, and social practice. MFA students have enrolled in courses offered in Physics, Asian Studies, Rhetoric, Film and Media, Peace and Conflict Studies, Art History, Anthropology, Music, Environmental Science, Botany, Mathematics, Middle-Eastern Studies, Religion, Classics, English, Architecture, Philosophy, Engineering, and Theatre, Dance and Performance Studies.</p>
<p><strong><em>TEACHING</em></strong></p>
<p>The MFA Program offers unique teaching opportunities. In their second year, MFA students are invited to teach their own section of our undergraduate foundation course, Art 8: “Introduction to Visual Thinking.” Opportunities exist to teach this course again, and/or other courses during summer sessions.</p>
<p><em><strong>PROFESSIONAL DEVELOPMENT</strong></em></p>
<p>We offer our MFA students professional development support, including privileged applicant status to a number of studio residency programs and grant programs. In consultation with a faculty advisor, each student will select and work closely with a thesis committee consisting of departmental and external members.</p>
<p><em><strong>STUDIO SPACE</strong></em></p>
<p>We offer our students semi-independent studio facilities, with each student matched to a studio that best suits their work. <a href="http://art.berkeley.edu/facilities-and-resources/graduate-studios/">Ten studios are located off-campus, at the Richmond Field Station</a>; two studios are located on-campus in Wurster Hall.</p>
<p><em><strong>EXHIBITION SPACES</strong></em></p>
<p>In addition to exhibiting in the Art Practice department’s Worth Ryder Art Gallery, MFA students are assisted in displaying their work in an array of regional venues. The second-year MFA Show is held at the newly-reopened Berkeley Art Museum, and students work closely with museum curators and staff to select and install their work.</p>
<p><em>**We have a very extensive Q&amp;A regarding our MFA program. You can find that page <a href="http://art.berkeley.edu/uc-berkeley-mfa-program-information/">here</a>.**</em></p>
	</div><!-- .entry-content-wrapper -->
</article><!-- #post-200 -->

						<div class="menu-programs-container"><ul id="menu-programs" class="menu"><li id="menu-item-1366" class="menu-item menu-item-type-post_type menu-item-object-page current-menu-item page_item page-item-63 current_page_item menu-item-1366"><a href="http://art.berkeley.edu/programs/undergraduate-program/">Undergraduate Program</a></li>
